### [FAIL] blogwatcher

- **Max Severity:** HIGH
- **Findings:** 2
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/blogwatcher

  #### [HIGH] Unpinned external dependency install via `go install ...@latest` (supply-chain risk)

  **Severity:** HIGH
  **Category:** supply_chain_attack
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill instructs users/agents to install a Go CLI directly from a remote GitHub repository using `@latest`. This creates a supply-chain risk: the fetched code can change over time, tags can be moved, and a compromised upstream repo or dependency could deliver malicious updates. Because the skill has no bundled scripts, the primary executable behavior is delegated to this externally retrieved binary, making provenance and integrity central to safety.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Install

  - Go: `go install github.com/Hyaxia/blogwatcher/cmd/blogwatcher@latest`
  ```

  **Remediation:** Pin to a specific immutable version (tag/commit) instead of `@latest` (e.g., `@vX.Y.Z` or a commit hash). Provide checksum/SLSA provenance guidance, document expected permissions/network behavior, and consider vendoring/releasing signed binaries.

  #### [LOW] Missing `allowed-tools` and `compatibility` metadata reduces enforceable tool restrictions

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest omits `allowed-tools` and does not specify compatibility. While optional, this reduces clarity about what tools the agent is expected to use and makes it harder to detect/stop unexpected tool usage (e.g., network access) during execution.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  - compatibility: Not specified
  - allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add `allowed-tools` (e.g., `[Bash]` if only shell execution is intended) and specify compatibility/assumptions (network required to fetch feeds, local storage locations, etc.).

### [OK] seo-review

- **Max Severity:** LOW
- **Findings:** 3
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/seo-review

  #### [LOW] Metadata quality issues reduce trust and can enable capability ambiguity

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE

  **Description:** The manifest omits optional but important fields (compatibility, allowed-tools) and includes a suspicious-looking date value (lastUpdated: '12026-02-25'), which may be a typo. While not a direct exploit, weak/ambiguous metadata can facilitate capability ambiguity during discovery/activation and makes it harder to verify tool restrictions and expected behavior.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  compatibility: Not specified; allowed-tools: Not specified; lastUpdated: '12026-02-25'
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit compatibility and allowed-tools declarations. Correct/validate metadata fields (e.g., lastUpdated) and consider adding versioning/provenance details that are internally consistent.

  #### [LOW] Referenced files missing (at.py, its.py) may indicate incomplete package or hidden behavior

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill references two files (at.py and its.py) that are not present in the provided package. Missing referenced artifacts can indicate an incomplete distribution, a packaging error, or an attempt to defer important logic to external/unavailable components (which can later be swapped in). While no scripts were found and the SKILL.md instruction body is empty here, the unresolved references reduce auditability and can become a tool-exploitation vector if the runtime resolves these names from an unexpected location (e.g., PATH/PYTHONPATH shadowing) in some agent/tooling environments.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced Files: at.py (not found), its.py (not found)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove the references if unused, or include the referenced files in the skill package. Ensure any execution/import paths are constrained to the skill directory to prevent accidental module/script shadowing. Add a minimal SKILL.md body describing intended behavior and how referenced files are used.

  **Severity:** INFO
  **Category:** policy_violation
  **Rule ID:** LLM_CONTEXT_BUDGET_EXCEEDED

  **Description:** instruction body (16,025 chars) exceeds limit (15,000)

  **Remediation:** Increase llm_analysis.max_instruction_body_chars in your scan policy to include this content in LLM analysis.

### [OK] openai-whisper-local

- **Max Severity:** LOW
- **Findings:** 2
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/openai-whisper-local

  #### [LOW] Potentially misleading 'entirely offline' claim depends on model download behavior

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** harmful_content
  **Rule ID:** LLM_HARMFUL_CONTENT
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The description states the skill runs 'entirely offline', but the instructions note models download to `~/.cache/whisper` on first run. While subsequent runs can be offline, first-run behavior may require network access depending on the local Whisper installation and model availability. This is not an exploit by itself, but it can mislead users about network exposure expectations.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Description: "runs entirely offline"
  Notes: "Models download to `~/.cache/whisper` on first run."
  ```

  **Remediation:** Clarify in the description/instructions that the first run may download model weights (network required unless pre-cached), and optionally provide steps for offline preloading or verifying cached models.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools and compatibility metadata (informational)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill manifest does not specify `allowed-tools` or `compatibility`. Per the framework, `allowed-tools` is optional, so this is not a policy violation; however, the absence of these fields reduces clarity about intended tool usage and execution environment constraints (e.g., whether Bash is expected for invoking the `whisper` CLI).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  YAML Manifest Details:
  - compatibility: Not specified
  - allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit `allowed-tools` (likely `[Bash]`, possibly `[Read, Write, Bash]` depending on expected file I/O) and `compatibility` to improve transparency and reduce accidental over-permissioning.

### [OK] ordercli

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 3
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/ordercli

  #### [MEDIUM] Encourages handling of sensitive credentials/tokens via CLI and environment variables without clear safeguards

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill instructions involve authenticating to Foodora using email and password via stdin and mention Deliveroo access via bearer token/cookie environment variables. While using --password-stdin is better than passing passwords on the command line, the skill provides no guidance on secure storage, redaction, or avoiding accidental logging/shell history capture for tokens/cookies. This increases the risk of credential exposure in terminal history, process listings (for other flags), logs, or shared config files—especially when combined with suggested config paths (e.g., /tmp) and browser cookie import flows.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  - `ordercli foodora login --email you@example.com --password-stdin`
  - "Requires `DELIVEROO_BEARER_TOKEN` (optional `DELIVEROO_COOKIE`)."
  - "Import Chrome cookies: `ordercli foodora cookies chrome --profile \"Default\"`"
  - "Use `--config /tmp/ordercli.json` for testing."
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit security guidance: avoid exporting tokens in shared shells; prefer OS keychain/credential store; warn about shell history and logging; recommend restrictive file permissions for config/session files; avoid /tmp for configs containing secrets; document how cookies/tokens are stored and how to revoke/clear them.

  #### [MEDIUM] Transitive trust risk via browser profile and cookie import/session extraction workflows

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill instructs users to reuse a browser profile directory and import Chrome cookies / create sessions from a browser profile. This pattern can cause the agent/user to rely on untrusted local browser state (cookies, sessions) and may inadvertently expose or reuse authentication material beyond the intended scope. While not an explicit prompt injection, it is an instruction-manipulation/transitive-trust risk because it encourages pulling sensitive auth artifacts from external state (browser profiles) without validation or least-privilege boundaries.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  - "Reuse profile: `--browser-profile \"$HOME/Library/Application Support/ordercli/browser-profile\"`"
  - "Import Chrome cookies: `ordercli foodora cookies chrome --profile \"Default\"`"
  - "Session import (no password)"
  ```

  **Remediation:** Document strict scoping: use a dedicated, minimal browser profile; avoid importing full cookie jars; clearly state what data is read and where it is stored; provide a secure deletion/cleanup procedure; require explicit user confirmation before any cookie/session extraction step.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools and compatibility metadata reduces enforceable safety boundaries

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest omits `allowed-tools` and `compatibility`. While optional, the absence of declared tool restrictions makes it harder to enforce least privilege for a skill that is explicitly about running a CLI that will handle authentication and potentially sensitive order/account data.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Manifest details indicate: `allowed-tools: Not specified`, `compatibility: Not specified`.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Declare `allowed-tools` explicitly (likely `Bash` only, possibly `Read` for config inspection) and document expected OS/network behavior and where credentials/sessions are stored.

### [OK] weather-cli

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 3
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/weather-cli

  #### [MEDIUM] Potential command injection via unescaped user-controlled location in curl examples

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** command_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill’s instructions encourage constructing curl commands by interpolating a user-provided location directly into the URL path/query (e.g., wttr.in/<Location>?format=...). If an agent follows these instructions using Bash and fails to properly quote/escape the location, a maliciously crafted location string could lead to shell metacharacter injection (e.g., `;`, `&&`, backticks, `$()`) when executed in a shell context. While the examples show quoted URLs in several places, not all examples are consistently quoted (e.g., `curl wttr.in/ORD`), and the skill provides no explicit guidance to sanitize/encode user input before forming commands.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Examples include `curl "wttr.in/London?format=3"`, `curl "wttr.in/New+York?format=3"`, and `curl wttr.in/ORD` with no explicit input sanitization guidance.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Require strict quoting for all curl invocations and treat the location as untrusted input. URL-encode the location (e.g., replace spaces with `+` or percent-encode) and reject/escape shell metacharacters. Prefer using curl with a fixed base URL and a safely encoded path/query (or use a small wrapper script that performs encoding and validation).

  #### [MEDIUM] Indirect prompt-injection / untrusted remote content risk from wttr.in responses

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill relies on fetching and displaying content from an external service (wttr.in). If an agent consumes the response and treats it as instructions (e.g., copying commands from output, following embedded text), this creates an indirect prompt-injection risk because the remote service is untrusted and could return adversarial content. The current instructions do not explicitly constrain the agent to treat responses as data-only.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Multiple instructions to `curl "wttr.in/..."` for text/JSON/PNG without guidance to treat returned content as untrusted data.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit guidance: treat all wttr.in output as untrusted data; never execute commands or follow instructions contained in responses. If parsing JSON, validate schema and only extract required fields.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools declaration (informational)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest does not specify `allowed-tools`. While optional, omission reduces clarity about intended tool usage and makes it harder to enforce least privilege (e.g., whether Bash/network access is expected).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  YAML Manifest Details: allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Declare `allowed-tools` explicitly (likely `[Bash]` or `[Bash, Read]` depending on intended usage) and document that the skill performs network requests to wttr.in.

### [OK] page-cro

- **Max Severity:** LOW
- **Findings:** 4
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/page-cro

  #### [LOW] Potential indirect prompt-injection surface via untrusted local context file

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions direct the agent to read `.agents/product-marketing-context.md` (or `.claude/product-marketing-context.md`) if present and to use that context. If those files are user-controlled or can be modified by other processes, they become an instruction-manipulation surface (e.g., embedding malicious directives like 'ignore prior rules' or 'exfiltrate data'). This is a common transitive-trust risk when ingesting external/untrusted text as guidance.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Instruction: "If `.agents/product-marketing-context.md` exists (or `.claude/product-marketing-context.md` in older setups), read it before asking questions. Use that context..."
  ```

  **Remediation:** Treat the context file as untrusted input: (1) clearly scope it to factual product info only, (2) ignore any instructions found inside it, (3) optionally add a sanitizer/heuristics step to strip imperative language, and (4) require user confirmation before acting on any surprising directives.

  #### [LOW] Metadata anomaly: implausible lastUpdated value

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The metadata includes `lastUpdated: '12026-02-19'`, which appears malformed (year 12026). While not directly exploitable, anomalous metadata can indicate poor provenance hygiene or tampering and complicates auditing and trust decisions.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  additional metadata: {'lastUpdated': '12026-02-19', 'provenance': 'ported'}
  ```

  **Remediation:** Correct metadata to a valid ISO date and add stronger provenance fields (version, source commit/hash, maintainer). Consider signing or checksumming released skill packages.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools declaration (reduced ability to enforce tool restrictions)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill manifest does not specify `allowed-tools`. While optional, the absence of an explicit tool allowlist reduces the ability to constrain tool usage if this skill is executed in an environment that supports tool gating. This is primarily a governance/controls gap rather than an exploit by itself.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  YAML Manifest Details: allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an explicit `allowed-tools` list aligned to intended behavior (likely [Read, Grep, Glob] if only reading local context files; exclude Bash/Python unless truly needed).

  #### [LOW] Broken/suspicious references in skill documentation (integrity and confusion risk)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill references multiple files that are not present (`templates/experiments.md`, `assets/experiments.md`, `the.py`). Missing references can cause agents to search elsewhere in the filesystem or substitute content, increasing the chance of unintended file access or tool misuse depending on the host agent’s behavior. The `the.py` reference is particularly odd for a CRO content skill and may indicate an incomplete or tampered package.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced Files: templates/experiments.md (not found), the.py (not found), assets/experiments.md (not found)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove unused references or include the intended files within the package. If any file is meant to be loaded, constrain reads to the skill directory and fail closed (do not search outside the package).

### [OK] ad-creative

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 5
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/ad-creative

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools/compatibility metadata reduces enforceability of tool restrictions

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE

  **Description:** The manifest omits allowed-tools and compatibility. While optional, the absence of allowed-tools makes it harder to enforce least privilege and to detect/stop unexpected tool use (e.g., filesystem reads outside the package, running CLIs). Given the skill’s references to external tooling and data pulls, explicit tool scoping would materially improve safety.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  allowed-tools: Not specified; compatibility: Not specified.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Declare allowed-tools explicitly (e.g., [Read] if purely instructional; or [Read, Grep] etc.). If the skill is intended to run CLIs, declare Bash and document required commands, plus require confirmation gates for any network/API interactions.

  #### [MEDIUM] Transitive trust risk: instructions encourage pulling/using external tool outputs and registries without validation

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill describes workflows that pull performance data via external CLIs/APIs and then 'feed winning patterns into this skill'. While not inherently malicious, this creates an indirect prompt-injection surface: untrusted CSV/API output (or content in external registry/integration docs) could contain adversarial strings that manipulate the agent’s behavior if treated as instructions rather than data. The skill does not include safeguards for treating such inputs as untrusted data, sanitizing, or isolating analysis from instruction-following.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "When the user provides performance data (CSV, paste, or API output), you analyze what's working..." and the workflow showing pulling data via node tools/clis/google-ads.js then analyzing and feeding into the skill.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit handling rules: treat CSV/API outputs as data only; never execute embedded instructions; strip/escape prompt-like content; summarize using fixed schemas; require user confirmation before any tool actions based on parsed data. If using external docs/registries, pin to vetted internal copies within the skill package.

  #### [MEDIUM] Path traversal / out-of-package references to tools registry and integrations

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill instructions reference multiple files via relative paths that traverse outside the skill package (e.g., ../../tools/...). In many agent runtimes, allowing a skill to read arbitrary relative paths can enable unintended access to host/project files (tool registries, configs, credentials, or other sensitive artifacts) and can also be used to influence behavior by pulling in unreviewed instructions from outside the skill boundary. Even if the current analyzer blocked these paths, the skill content is written to rely on them, which is a security risk in less restrictive environments.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  References include: ../../tools/REGISTRY.md and ../../tools/integrations/{google-ads,meta-ads,linkedin-ads,tiktok-ads}.md (noted as blocked: path traversal attempt).
  ```

  **Remediation:** Constrain references to files within the skill directory (e.g., ./references/...). If external registries are required, expose them through a vetted, sandboxed interface rather than filesystem traversal. Add explicit guidance: do not read files outside the skill package; require user confirmation before accessing any project files.

  #### [INFO] 'references/generative-tools.md' excluded from LLM analysis (23,826 chars)

  **Severity:** INFO
  **Category:** policy_violation
  **Rule ID:** LLM_CONTEXT_BUDGET_EXCEEDED
  **Location:** references/generative-tools.md

  **Description:** file size (23,826 chars) exceeds per-file limit (8,000)

  **Remediation:** Increase llm_analysis.max_referenced_file_chars in your scan policy to include this content in LLM analysis.

  #### [MEDIUM] Suspicious free domain URL in asset

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** policy_violation
  **Rule ID:** ASSET_SUSPICIOUS_URL
  **Location:** references/generative-tools.md:91

  **Description:** Pattern 'https://docs.bfl.ml/...' detected in asset file

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  **Docs:** [Replicate Flux](https://replicate.com/black-forest-labs/flux-2-pro), [BFL API](https://do
  ```

  **Remediation:** Review the asset file and remove any malicious or unnecessary dynamic patterns

  **Description:** The skill’s referenced-files list includes `the.py`, but the package inventory indicates only markdown files are present and `the.py` is not found. While no executable scripts are present (reducing direct code-execution risk), missing or inconsistent references can cause agents to fail open (e.g., improvising behavior), or can be a sign of incomplete/incorrect packaging that later gets “fixed” by adding scripts without review. This is primarily a reliability and governance concern, with minor security implications if future versions introduce executable content under the same reference.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```

  ```

  #### [MEDIUM] File extension does not match actual content type

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** obfuscation
  **Rule ID:** FILE_MAGIC_MISMATCH
  **Location:** examples/general-comms.md

  **Description:** File 'general-comms.md' extension (.md) suggests one format but Magika detected a different text format: YAML source (yaml). This may indicate content obfuscation or a misnamed file.

  **Remediation:** Rename the file to match its actual content type, or remove it if it appears malicious.

  #### [LOW] File type mismatch flagged by static analyzer (content/extension inconsistency)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** obfuscation
  **Rule ID:** LLM_OBFUSCATION
  **Location:** examples/general-comms.md

  **Description:** Static analysis reports a file type mismatch for `examples/general-comms.md` (extension does not match detected content). This can be benign (e.g., unusual formatting), but content/extension mismatches are also a common obfuscation technique to hide non-markdown payloads or to confuse downstream tooling that assumes markdown is safe. Given the inventory shows only markdown files and no scripts, impact is currently limited, but it warrants manual inspection of the file contents.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Pre-Scan Context: File type mismatches (extension != content): examples/general-comms.md
  ```

  **Remediation:** Manually inspect `examples/general-comms.md` to confirm it is plain markdown (no embedded executable code blocks intended for execution, no hidden binary/encoded blobs). Ensure repository tooling enforces correct MIME/type and blocks non-text content in .md files.

Missing or inconsistent references can cause agents to fall back to alternative behaviors (e.g., improvising steps, searching broadly for similarly named files), which can increase the chance of unintended file access or mis-activation.   **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill manifest does not specify `allowed-tools`. While optional, the absence of explicit tool scoping makes it harder to enforce least-privilege expectations (e.g., ensuring the skill remains read-only and does not unexpectedly use Bash/Python or write files when integrated into an agent runtime).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  YAML Manifest Details: allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an explicit `allowed-tools` list aligned to intended behavior (likely [Read] and possibly [Write] only if theme generation is meant to persist files). Document any required tool usage.

  **Location:** theme-showcase.pdf

  **Description:** Binary file 'theme-showcase.pdf' cannot be inspected by the scanner. Reason: Binary file (.pdf) - cannot inspect content. Binary files resist static analysis and may contain hidden functionality.

### [OK] notion-api

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 2
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/notion-api

  #### [MEDIUM] Sensitive credential handling guidance stores Notion API key in plaintext file

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill instructs users to store the Notion API key in a plaintext file under ~/.config/notion/api_key and then read it into an environment variable for curl usage. While common in quickstart docs, this increases the risk of accidental exposure (e.g., overly permissive file permissions, backups, logs, shell history, or other tools reading the file). This is a data exposure risk pattern, especially for an agent skill that may be used in automated workflows.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  mkdir -p ~/.config/notion
  echo "ntn_your_key_here" > ~/.config/notion/api_key

  NOTION_KEY=$(cat ~/.config/notion/api_key)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Recommend safer secret storage practices: (1) use OS keychain/secret manager (macOS Keychain, Windows Credential Manager, GNOME Keyring) or a dedicated CLI secret store; (2) if a file must be used, instruct setting restrictive permissions (e.g., chmod 600 ~/.config/notion/api_key) and avoiding echoing secrets in shell history; (3) prefer environment variables injected at runtime (CI secrets) rather than persisted plaintext; (4) add explicit warnings about not committing or sharing the key.

  #### [LOW] Manifest metadata quality issues (missing allowed-tools/compatibility; suspicious lastUpdated value)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest details indicate allowed-tools and compatibility are not specified, reducing the ability to enforce least-privilege tool use. Additionally, the lastUpdated value appears malformed ("12026-01-31"), which can indicate provenance/porting issues and complicate auditing. These are not direct exploits but weaken governance and review.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  compatibility: Not specified
  allowed-tools: Not specified
  lastUpdated: '12026-01-31'
  provenance: 'ported'
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit allowed-tools (e.g., [Bash] if only curl examples are intended) and compatibility notes. Correct/validate metadata fields (lastUpdated) and include versioning/provenance details to support auditing.

  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE

  **Description:** The skill description includes a long list of trigger phrases (e.g., "chat bot", multiple platform names, and package identifiers). While not overtly malicious, broad keyword baiting can increase accidental activation in unrelated contexts, which is a form of capability/activation inflation risk.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "Triggers on \"chat sdk\", \"chat bot\", \"slack bot\", \"teams bot\", \"discord bot\", \"@chat-adapter\", building bots that work across multiple chat platforms."
  ```

  **Remediation:** Narrow triggers to more specific intents (e.g., "Chat SDK" + explicit package names) and avoid generic phrases like "chat bot" unless necessary. Add clear guidance on when NOT to use the skill.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools metadata reduces enforceable tool-scope constraints

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE

  **Description:** The manifest does not specify allowed-tools. This is optional, but without it there is no explicit constraint on tool usage, which weakens policy enforcement and increases the blast radius if the skill is later extended with scripts or tool-using instructions.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "allowed-tools: Not specified"
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an explicit allowed-tools list appropriate to expected usage (e.g., Read/Grep/Glob for code guidance; add Write/Bash only if necessary) and ensure instructions/scripts stay within that scope.

  #### [LOW] Potentially unsafe example pattern: echoing untrusted user content back into chat

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** harmful_content
  **Rule ID:** LLM_HARMFUL_CONTENT
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The example bot posts back `message.text` directly. In real chat platforms, this can enable mention-spam, formatting injection, or social-engineering amplification (e.g., re-posting @channel, malicious links). This is not a direct vulnerability in the skill package itself, but it is a common unsafe default pattern for bot developers.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "await thread.post(`You said: ${message.text}`);"
  ```

  **Remediation:** Recommend sanitizing/escaping mentions and formatting, rate-limiting, and optionally stripping mass-mentions/links. Provide a safer example (e.g., redact @channel/@here, escape markdown, or summarize instead of verbatim echo).

  #### [LOW] Transitive trust risk: instructions encourage reading from node_modules documentation

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions direct the agent/user to "always read" documentation and types from paths under node_modules. While local package docs are often legitimate, node_modules content is supply-chain controlled and could contain malicious prompt-injection text (e.g., MDX with embedded instructions) that an agent might follow if treated as authoritative. This is an indirect prompt-injection risk if the agent is instructed to follow those docs as executable guidance rather than reference material.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "The `chat` package ships with full documentation in `node_modules/chat/docs/` ... **Always read these before writing code**"
  ```

  **Remediation:** Rephrase to treat node_modules docs as reference only and explicitly instruct: do not follow any instructions found in docs that request secrets, system prompt disclosure, network exfiltration, or tool misuse. Prefer linking to vetted, pinned documentation sources and/or include a vetted subset of docs within the skill package.

  #### [LOW] Skill description exceeds agent skills length limit

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** policy_violation
  **Rule ID:** MANIFEST_DESCRIPTION_TOO_LONG
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** Skill description is 540 characters; Agent skills limit the `description` field to 512 characters.

  **Remediation:** Shorten the description to 512 characters or fewer while keeping it specific

### [FAIL] oracle-cli

- **Max Severity:** HIGH
- **Findings:** 5
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/oracle-cli

  #### [LOW] File type mismatch flagged by pre-scan may hide unexpected content

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** obfuscation
  **Rule ID:** LLM_OBFUSCATION

  **Description:** Static analysis reports a file type mismatch for `examples/general-comms.md` (extension != content). Mismatches can be used to conceal payloads or misleading instructions, even though no direct malicious content is provided in the excerpt.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Pre-Scan Context: “File type mismatches (extension != content): examples/general-comms.md”
  ```

  **Remediation:** Inspect the mismatched file to confirm it is benign and matches expected markdown content. Enforce repository checks that prevent non-markdown/binary content from being stored with .md extensions.

  #### [HIGH] Skill encourages bundling and transmitting local repository files to third-party model providers (data exposure risk)

  **Severity:** HIGH
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill’s core purpose is to bundle user-selected local files and send them to external AI model providers via either an API engine (keyed by environment variables like OPENAI_API_KEY) or a browser automation engine. This creates a high risk of inadvertent disclosure of sensitive information (secrets in source, proprietary code, credentials in config files, internal URLs, etc.). While the instructions include a brief safety note (“Don’t attach secrets by default”), the overall workflow normalizes uploading repo context and includes features like remote browser hosting that can further expand the exposure surface if misconfigured.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  “Oracle bundles your prompt + selected files into one “one-shot” request so another model can answer with real repo context (API or browser automation).”
  “Auto-pick: `api` when `OPENAI_API_KEY` is set; otherwise `browser`.”
  “Remote browser host: `oracle serve --host 0.0.0.0 --port 9473 --token <secret>` … `--remote-host <host:port> --remote-token <secret>`”
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit, prominent guardrails: (1) default-deny patterns for common secret files (.env, _credentials_, \*.pem, id_rsa, .npmrc, .pypirc, kubeconfig, etc.) and require an explicit override to include them; (2) a preflight scanner that detects likely secrets and blocks or redacts by default; (3) require user confirmation showing an exact file list + size/token estimate before any network/browser upload; (4) document the security implications of remote hosting and recommend binding to localhost by default (127.0.0.1) with strong tokens and TLS when remote.

  #### [MEDIUM] Remote host binding to 0.0.0.0 increases attack surface (token interception/unauthorized access risk)

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** command_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill documentation suggests running a remote browser host bound to 0.0.0.0. Exposing a service on all interfaces can enable unauthorized access attempts, token brute forcing, or token leakage (e.g., via shell history, logs, or process lists). This is a security-sensitive operational pattern even if a token is used.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  “oracle serve --host 0.0.0.0 --port 9473 --token <secret>”
  ```

  **Remediation:** Change documentation defaults to bind to 127.0.0.1 unless remote access is explicitly required. Recommend TLS, strong randomly generated tokens, avoiding token exposure in shell history (use env vars or prompt), and firewall rules/IP allowlists when remote.

  #### [MEDIUM] Potential availability/compute risk from long-running browser sessions and reattachment workflow

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** resource_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions describe long-running browser-based runs (10 minutes to 1 hour) and session persistence/reattachment. While not inherently malicious, this pattern can lead to resource exhaustion (multiple concurrent sessions, repeated retries, or unattended long runs), especially in shared environments or CI-like contexts where users may trigger repeated runs.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  “This is the common “long think” path: ~10 minutes to ~1 hour is normal; expect a stored session you can reattach to.”
  “Runs may detach or take a long time… If the CLI times out: don’t re-run; reattach.”
  ```

  **Remediation:** Document and enforce sensible limits: maximum concurrent sessions, timeouts with clear cancellation, and guidance to avoid running in automated loops. Provide a clear ‘stop/cancel session’ command and recommend quotas for shared machines.

  #### [LOW] Capability inflation/ambiguity: broad multi-provider claims without manifest tool constraints

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill description claims broad capabilities (browser automation, API engines, multiple model providers, session management) but the manifest omits `allowed-tools` and compatibility details. This increases ambiguity about what tools/network actions are expected and can lead to over-trust or unintended activation in environments that rely on manifest constraints.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Manifest: “allowed-tools: Not specified”, “compatibility: Not specified”
  Description: “Supports browser and API engines, session management, and multiple model providers…”
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add `allowed-tools` and explicit compatibility/network expectations in the manifest (e.g., requires network/browser automation; reads local files; may store sessions under ~/.oracle). Clarify what data leaves the machine and under what conditions.

### [OK] video-frames

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 3
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/video-frames

  #### [LOW] Referenced file appears missing/inconsistent (a.py)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions/reference list indicates a referenced file (a.py) that is not present. This can cause confusion during execution and may indicate packaging errors or leftover references from porting, which can be abused in some ecosystems for shadowing/ambiguity (e.g., if a similarly named file is later introduced).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced Files: Files referenced in instructions: a.py (not found)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove the stale reference or include the intended file. Ensure referenced resources are present and integrity-checked as part of packaging.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools metadata reduces ability to enforce tool restrictions

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest does not specify allowed-tools. While optional, omission makes it harder to enforce least-privilege expectations for the skill (e.g., it clearly requires Bash and filesystem write access to the output path).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an explicit allowed-tools list (e.g., [Bash, Read, Write]) consistent with the skill’s behavior.

  #### [MEDIUM] Potential ffmpeg filter injection via unvalidated --index argument

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** command_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION
  **Location:** scripts/frame.sh

  **Description:** The script interpolates the user-supplied --index value directly into an ffmpeg filter expression: -vf "select=eq(n\,${index})". While the shell invocation itself is quoted (reducing classic shell injection risk), ffmpeg has its own expression language and filtergraph parsing. If an attacker can supply crafted values (e.g., including characters that alter the expression), this may lead to unexpected behavior, errors, or potentially exploitation of ffmpeg parsing vulnerabilities depending on the installed ffmpeg build. At minimum, it is an input-validation weakness that can be abused to break expected operation.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  ffmpeg ... -vf "select=eq(n\\,${index})" ...
  ```

  **Remediation:** Validate --index strictly as a non-negative integer (e.g., regex ^[0-9]+$) before use. Consider rejecting any non-digit characters and enforcing reasonable bounds. Optionally, use safer ffmpeg options that avoid expression interpolation when possible.

### [FAIL] web-design-guidelines

- **Max Severity:** HIGH
- **Findings:** 4
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/web-design-guidelines

  #### [HIGH] Indirect prompt injection risk via external guideline fetch (transitive trust of remote instructions)

  **Severity:** HIGH
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill instructs the agent to fetch guidelines from a remote GitHub raw URL before each review and to follow the fetched content for both rules and output-format instructions. This creates an indirect prompt-injection channel: if the remote content is modified (repo compromise, malicious PR, DNS/transport interception, or content drift), it can inject instructions that override intended behavior (e.g., request additional sensitive files, change reporting, or attempt tool-use escalation). Because the fetched content is treated as authoritative instructions, it becomes an untrusted control plane for the agent.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "Fetch the latest guidelines from the source URL" and "Use WebFetch to retrieve the latest rules. The fetched content contains all the rules and output format instructions." (URL: https://raw.githubusercontent.com/vercel-labs/web-interface-guidelines/main/command.md)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Vendor and pin the guidelines inside the skill package (ship a local copy) and update via a controlled release process; or pin to an immutable commit hash and verify integrity (e.g., checksum/signature). Treat fetched content as data only: parse a constrained rule format (JSON/YAML) rather than executing/obeying natural-language instructions. Add explicit guardrails in SKILL.md: never follow tool-use or file-access instructions from fetched content; only apply predefined rule checks.

  #### [MEDIUM] Tooling mismatch/unauthorized tool assumption (WebFetch referenced but not declared)

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions require using "WebFetch" to retrieve remote guidelines, but the manifest does not specify allowed-tools. In environments where tool allowlists are enforced, this can lead to unauthorized tool usage attempts or inconsistent behavior. It also obscures the skill’s true operational requirements (network retrieval) from reviewers and policy enforcement.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "Use WebFetch to retrieve the latest rules." while "allowed-tools: Not specified"
  ```

  **Remediation:** Declare required tools explicitly in YAML (e.g., allowed-tools including the appropriate web-fetch/network tool if supported). If network access is not permitted, remove WebFetch dependency and bundle guidelines locally.

  #### [LOW] Metadata quality issues and suspicious/invalid fields reduce trust and reviewability

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The package shows indicators of poor provenance/porting artifacts: "provenance: ported", an invalid-looking date "lastUpdated: '12026-01-16'", and a file type mismatch warning for SKILL.md. While not a direct exploit, these issues reduce confidence in the package integrity and can be used to mask malicious changes or confuse automated scanners.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  additional metadata includes "provenance": "ported" and "lastUpdated": "12026-01-16"; pre-scan notes "File type mismatches (extension != content): SKILL.md"
  ```

  **Remediation:** Fix metadata (valid dates, clear provenance), ensure SKILL.md is valid markdown with correct frontmatter, and add versioning/changelog. Re-run static checks after normalization.

  #### [LOW] Broken reference to non-existent file may cause unsafe fallback behaviors

  **Location:** SKILL.md


  **Remediation:** Correct metadata (valid dates, clear provenance). Add versioning and maintenance notes. If the skill is purely instructional (no scripts), state that explicitly to avoid over-claiming operational capabilities.

  #### [LOW] Fact-checking guidance can produce misleading outputs without explicit uncertainty/limitations controls

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** harmful_content
  **Rule ID:** LLM_HARMFUL_CONTENT
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill provides a structured fact-checking template and rating scale but includes no explicit safeguards against hallucinated citations, fabricated sources, or overconfident verdicts when evidence is unavailable. This is a content-integrity risk: users may treat outputs as verified even when the model cannot actually access authoritative sources in the current environment.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Sections: "Verification Process", "Rating Scale", and "Sources" template encourage citation output but do not require verifiable retrieval or explicit 'no-browse/no-access' disclosure.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add instructions to (1) clearly state browsing/tooling limitations, (2) avoid inventing citations, (3) prefer 'UNVERIFIABLE' when sources cannot be checked, and (4) request user-provided sources or enable a vetted retrieval toolchain if available.

  #### [LOW] Referenced files missing (early.py, opinion.py) may indicate incomplete package or hidden behavior

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The SKILL.md references two Python files (early.py, opinion.py) that are not present in the provided package. While missing references are often benign (documentation drift), they can also indicate an incomplete/ported skill, or an attempt to imply functionality that is not actually shipped for review. This creates review gaps and can mask intended tool use or logic that would otherwise be auditable.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced Files:
  - early.py (not found)
  - opinion.py (not found)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove the references if they are not required, or include the files in the package. If they are optional, explicitly mark them as optional and describe their purpose and expected behavior.

### [FAIL] things-mac-cli

- **Max Severity:** HIGH
- **Findings:** 5
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/things-mac-cli

  #### [HIGH] Unpinned third-party installation via `go install ...@latest` (supply-chain risk)

  **Severity:** HIGH
  **Category:** supply_chain_attack
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill instructs users/agents to install a third-party CLI directly from GitHub using `go install ...@latest`. Using `@latest` is effectively unpinned and can pull different code over time (including a compromised release/tag), creating a supply-chain compromise risk. Because this skill has no bundled scripts, the primary executable behavior is delegated to this external dependency, increasing the impact of upstream compromise.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Install (recommended, Apple Silicon): `GOBIN=/opt/homebrew/bin go install github.com/ossianhempel/things3-cli/cmd/things@latest`
  ```

  **Remediation:** Pin to a specific version/tag/commit (e.g., `@vX.Y.Z`), document integrity verification (checksums/signatures where applicable), and provide guidance for reviewing the upstream repository/release notes before upgrading.

  #### [MEDIUM] Encourages granting Full Disk Access to enable local database reads (over-privilege / data exposure risk)

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions recommend granting Full Disk Access to the calling app to resolve database read failures. Full Disk Access significantly broadens what the invoking process (Terminal or an AI gateway app) can read, increasing the blast radius if the process, the installed CLI, or any other invoked tooling is compromised. While reading the Things database is part of the stated purpose, the permission change is broad and can expose unrelated sensitive user data.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  If DB reads fail: grant **Full Disk Access** to the calling app (Terminal for manual runs; `the AI assistant.app` for gateway runs).
  ```

  **Remediation:** Prefer least-privilege guidance: document the exact file paths needed, suggest using a dedicated minimal-permission runner where possible, and warn users to avoid granting Full Disk Access to broad/complex apps (especially AI gateway apps) unless strictly necessary.

  #### [MEDIUM] Auth token handling guidance may lead to accidental secret exposure

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill suggests passing `--auth-token <TOKEN>` on the command line and/or setting `THINGS_AUTH_TOKEN`. Command-line tokens can be exposed via shell history, process listings, logs, or telemetry. Environment variables can also leak via crash reports, debug logs, or child processes. This is a common secret-handling pitfall and can result in credential exposure.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Optional: set `THINGS_AUTH_TOKEN` to avoid passing `--auth-token` for update ops.
  ... `things update --id <UUID> --auth-token <TOKEN> ...`
  ```

  **Remediation:** Recommend safer secret handling: prefer reading tokens from a secure keychain/credential store, avoid CLI flags for secrets, document how to prevent shell history logging (or use `read -s` prompts), and caution against sharing logs/terminal output containing tokens.

  #### [LOW] Static analyzer reports file type mismatch for SKILL.md (review for hidden/embedded content)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** obfuscation
  **Rule ID:** LLM_OBFUSCATION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** A pre-scan indicates a file type mismatch for SKILL.md (extension != content). This can indicate embedded non-markdown content or obfuscation (e.g., hidden payloads, binary/HTML/script content) and warrants manual inspection of the raw file bytes to confirm it is plain text markdown and contains only the documented instructions.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Pre-Scan Context: File type mismatches (extension != content): SKILL.md
  ```

  **Remediation:** Open SKILL.md in a hex/text viewer, confirm it is UTF-8 text markdown, and remove any embedded/hidden content. Ensure repository tooling enforces text-only for SKILL.md (e.g., pre-commit checks).

  #### [LOW] Missing `allowed-tools` and `compatibility` metadata reduces enforceable tool-scope clarity

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest omits `allowed-tools` and does not specify compatibility. While optional, the absence of explicit tool scoping makes it harder for an agent host to enforce least privilege and for reviewers to quickly validate intended tool usage (e.g., whether Bash execution and package installation are expected).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  allowed-tools: Not specified
  compatibility: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add `allowed-tools` (e.g., likely `Bash` only, if the skill is purely CLI usage) and specify compatibility/assumptions (macOS version, Things 3 installed, whether network access is required for installation).

There are no guardrails (timeouts, size checks, confirmation prompts) before running potentially expensive operations. In agentic contexts, this can be abused to degrade availability (e.g., repeated runs, large projects).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Multiple `pnpm install`/`pnpm add` operations and `pnpm exec parcel build ...` with no limits or prompts.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit user confirmation before installs/builds, implement basic checks (disk space, project size), and document expected resource usage. In agent runtimes, enforce execution timeouts and rate limits.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools metadata reduces enforceable tool scoping

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE

  **Description:** The manifest omits `allowed-tools`. While optional, the skill clearly requires Bash execution and filesystem writes. Without explicit tool scoping, host platforms cannot easily enforce least-privilege or detect tool misuse relative to declared intent.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Manifest details indicate `allowed-tools: Not specified` while instructions require running bash scripts that write many files and install dependencies.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Declare `allowed-tools` explicitly (at minimum: `[Bash, Write, Read]`, and possibly `Grep/Glob` if needed). Document that the skill performs package installation and project scaffolding.

  #### [HIGH] Unpinned/remote dependency installation via pnpm enables supply-chain compromise

  **Severity:** HIGH
  **Category:** supply_chain_attack
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK
  **Location:** scripts/bundle-artifact.sh

  **Description:** The skill’s scripts install numerous npm packages at runtime (including some without version pins and one explicitly set to "latest"). This creates a large supply-chain attack surface: a compromised upstream package, malicious new release, or dependency confusion event could execute arbitrary code during install (npm lifecycle scripts) or at build time. Because the skill is intended to be run locally, this is a meaningful risk even if the skill itself contains no explicit exfiltration logic.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  init-artifact.sh: `npm install -g pnpm`; `pnpm create vite ...`; `VITE_VERSION="latest"` for Node>=20; many `pnpm install ...` lines with mostly unpinned versions.
  bundle-artifact.sh: `pnpm add -D parcel @parcel/config-default parcel-resolver-tspaths html-inline` (no version pins).
  ```

  **Remediation:** Pin exact versions for all installed packages (including devDependencies) and avoid using "latest". Prefer a lockfile committed with verified integrity (pnpm-lock.yaml) and use `pnpm install --frozen-lockfile`. Consider vendoring a known-good template or using a trusted internal registry/proxy with allowlisting.

  #### [MEDIUM] Potential command/argument injection via unsanitized project name passed to tooling

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** command_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION
  **Location:** scripts/init-artifact.sh

  **Description:** The initialization script accepts a user-controlled `<project-name>` and passes it directly to `pnpm create vite` and later interpolates it into output messages and an HTML title replacement. While the variable is quoted in the shell invocation (reducing classic shell metacharacter injection), it can still cause unsafe behavior such as path traversal (e.g., `../somewhere`), creation of unexpected directories, or surprising argument-like values depending on downstream tool parsing. Additionally, the sed-based HTML title substitution uses the raw project name, which could inject markup into `index.html` (e.g., `<script>` in the title) if a user supplies a malicious name.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  PROJECT_NAME="$1"; `pnpm create vite "$PROJECT_NAME" --template react-ts`; `$SED_INPLACE 's/<title>.*<\/title>/<title>'"$PROJECT_NAME"'<\/title>/' index.html`
  ```

  **Remediation:** Validate/normalize `PROJECT_NAME` (e.g., allow only `[a-zA-Z0-9-_]`, disallow path separators and `..`). For HTML title updates, escape special characters or use a safer templating approach. Consider rejecting names that start with `-` or contain whitespace/control characters.

  #### [MEDIUM] Archive file detected in skill package

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** policy_violation
  **Rule ID:** ARCHIVE_FILE_DETECTED
  **Location:** scripts/shadcn-components.tar.gz

  **Description:** Archive file found: scripts/shadcn-components.tar.gz. Archives can contain hidden executables, scripts, or other malicious content that is not visible without extraction.

  **Remediation:** Extract archive contents and include files directly, or document the archive's purpose.

  #### [MEDIUM] Unanalyzable binary file

  **Category:** policy_violation
  **Rule ID:** UNANALYZABLE_BINARY
  **Location:** scripts/shadcn-components.tar.gz

  **Description:** Binary file 'scripts/shadcn-components.tar.gz' cannot be inspected by the scanner. Reason: Binary file (.gz) - cannot inspect content. Binary files resist static analysis and may contain hidden functionality.

  **Remediation:** Replace binary files with source code, or submit the binary to VirusTotal for independent verification (--use-virustotal).

### [OK] content-design

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 3
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/content-design

  #### [LOW] Manifest omissions reduce enforceable tool constraints

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE

  **Description:** The manifest does not specify "allowed-tools" or "compatibility". Per the provided framework this is low severity, but it weakens policy enforcement and makes it harder to verify that the skill will remain within intended tool boundaries (especially given the Audit mode guidance to use search tools).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  - compatibility: Not specified
  - allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an explicit allowed-tools list (e.g., [Read, Grep, Glob] for audit-only workflows) and document any expected tool usage. Specify compatibility expectations if relevant.

  #### [MEDIUM] Audit mode encourages broad file scanning without explicit scope/consent guardrails

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill’s Audit mode instructs scanning a file or set of files (UI components, i18n JSON/YAML) and provides grep patterns to run across relevant files. Without explicit scoping guidance (project subdirectory boundaries, file allowlists, exclusions for secrets, confirmation before scanning large repos), this can lead to over-collection of content and accidental exposure of sensitive strings (API keys in configs, internal URLs, credentials in env files) during analysis output.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "**Audit** -- Scan a file or set of files (UI components, i18n JSON/YAML) for violations. Use search tools to find patterns, then report."

  "When running Audit mode, use grep patterns against i18n files and UI components... Run each pattern against the relevant files"
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit scope controls: require the user to specify target directories/files; exclude common secret locations (.env, credentials files, node_modules, build artifacts); cap the number of matches shown; redact likely secrets; and require confirmation before scanning outside a specified path.

  #### [LOW] Referenced file missing (potential packaging/tooling issue)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** SKILL.md references a file named "the.py", but it is not present in the provided package. While not inherently malicious, missing referenced files can cause the agent to fall back to alternative behaviors (e.g., improvising steps, using broader searches, or invoking tools unexpectedly), and it reduces auditability of the intended workflow.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced Files:
  Files referenced in instructions: the.py

  **Referenced File: the.py** (not found)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove the reference if unintended, or include the referenced file in the package. If the file is meant to be optional, explicitly document the fallback behavior and ensure it does not expand tool usage unexpectedly.

### [OK] fullstack-developer

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 4
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/fullstack-developer

  #### [MEDIUM] Opaque bundled archive content indicated by static inventory (potential hidden/obfuscated payloads)

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** obfuscation
  **Rule ID:** LLM_OBFUSCATION

  **Description:** Static inventory indicates a tar.gz archive with TypeScript files inside (e.g., `scripts/shadcn-components.tar.gz!/lib/utils.ts`). Bundled archives can conceal additional code that is not easily reviewed in normal diffs and may be used to hide malicious logic (obfuscation/evasion). The provided input does not include the archive contents, so maliciousness cannot be confirmed, but the packaging pattern warrants review.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Pre-scan: "unreferenced_scripts": ['scripts/shadcn-components.tar.gz!/lib/utils.ts', 'scripts/shadcn-components.tar.gz!/hooks/use-toast.ts'] and file types include a binary and bash scripts not shown.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Unpack and review the tar.gz contents; ensure all shipped code is visible and referenced. Add CI checks to forbid opaque archives or require checksum + review. Remove unreferenced scripts or document their purpose. Review any included bash scripts for unsafe operations.

  #### [MEDIUM] Supply-chain risk via recommended package installation without version pinning

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** supply_chain_attack
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill’s examples instruct installing dependencies using npm and npx (e.g., `npm install @prisma/client zod`, `npx prisma ...`) without version pinning or integrity controls. This is a common supply-chain risk pattern: users may install compromised or typosquatted packages, or receive breaking/malicious updates. While this is example content (not an executable script in the skill), it can still lead to real-world compromise if followed.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "npm install @prisma/client zod" and "npm install -D prisma" and multiple "npx prisma ..." commands.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Recommend version pinning (e.g., `npm install zod@3.23.8 prisma@5.XX.X`), use lockfiles, enable npm audit, consider `npm ci`, and advise verifying package provenance. For `npx`, recommend `npx prisma@<version>` to avoid pulling unexpected latest versions.

  #### [LOW] Over-broad capability description may cause unintended activation (capability inflation)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill is named and described as a general "Full-Stack Developer" covering a wide range of technologies and tasks (frontend, backend, database, DevOps). While not inherently malicious, this breadth can increase the chance of the skill being selected/activated for many unrelated requests, which is a common discovery/activation abuse pattern (capability inflation). No explicit keyword-baiting or deceptive impersonation is present, but the scope is very broad relative to a typical narrowly-scoped skill.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "You are an expert full-stack web developer specializing in modern JavaScript/TypeScript stacks with React, Node.js, and databases." + extensive "When to Apply" list including deployment/scaling/third-party integrations.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Narrow the scope (e.g., split into separate skills for frontend, backend, database, DevOps) and add clearer activation criteria/constraints (what it will not do, required user confirmation for risky steps like deployment, secrets handling).

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools metadata reduces enforceable tool-safety boundaries

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest does not specify `allowed-tools`. Per the provided framework this is optional and therefore low severity, but it reduces clarity/enforceability of what tools the skill is intended to use (e.g., Bash/Python/Write). This matters because the skill includes instructions that could lead to running package managers and deployment commands in other contexts.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "allowed-tools: Not specified"
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an explicit `allowed-tools` list aligned to intended behavior (e.g., if it is advisory-only, omit Bash/Write; if it may generate commands, still constrain execution and require confirmation steps).

### [FAIL] openai-image-gen

- **Max Severity:** CRITICAL
- **Findings:** 9
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/openai-image-gen

  #### [MEDIUM] Tooling/manifest mismatch: allowed-tools not specified despite requiring network and filesystem writes

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest omits allowed-tools, but the skill’s operation inherently requires network access (HTTPS requests) and filesystem writes (creating output directories, writing images/HTML/JSON). In agent ecosystems where allowed-tools is used to constrain tool use, omission reduces policy clarity and can lead to over-permissive execution or review gaps.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  YAML Manifest Details: allowed-tools: Not specified

  scripts/gen.py writes files and performs network requests.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an explicit allowed-tools declaration aligned to behavior (e.g., [Python, Bash] at minimum; and in platforms that model network/file tools separately, declare network + write permissions). Also document output locations and what files are created.

  #### [MEDIUM] Undeclared network usage

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** TOOL_ABUSE_UNDECLARED_NETWORK
  **Location:** catalog/skills/openai-image-gen/SKILL.md

  **Description:** Skill code uses network libraries but doesn't declare network requirement

  **Remediation:** Declare network usage in compatibility field or remove network calls

  #### [MEDIUM] Environment variable harvesting detected

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_HARVESTING
  **Location:** catalog/skills/openai-image-gen/scripts/gen.py

  **Description:** Script iterates through environment variables in catalog/skills/openai-image-gen/scripts/gen.py

  **Remediation:** Remove environment variable collection unless explicitly required and documented

  #### [MEDIUM] Network egress to OpenAI API and remote URL download may expose prompts/metadata and expands data-flow risk

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** scripts/gen.py

  **Description:** The skill sends user-provided (or randomly generated) prompts to the OpenAI Images API over the network. This is expected for an image-generation skill, but it is still a data-exposure surface: prompts may contain sensitive information if users include it. Additionally, the script supports a response mode where the API returns a URL and the script downloads that URL via urlretrieve; while the URL is expected to be OpenAI-controlled, this introduces a secondary network fetch step and a broader egress/data-flow surface if the response were ever manipulated (e.g., via proxy/MITM in compromised environments) or if the API behavior changes.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  url = "https://api.openai.com/v1/images/generations" ... urllib.request.urlopen(req, timeout=300)
  ...
  image_url = data.get("url")
  ...
  urllib.request.urlretrieve(image_url, filepath)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Document clearly that prompts are transmitted to OpenAI and should not include secrets. Prefer requesting/using base64 responses only (avoid secondary URL downloads) if supported, or validate/allowlist the download host (e.g., only https and only known OpenAI domains) before fetching. Consider adding an explicit confirmation step when prompts are sourced from user input in agent contexts.

  #### [LOW] Potential compute/cost exhaustion via unbounded --count and large image sizes

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** resource_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE
  **Location:** scripts/gen.py

  **Description:** The script allows generating an arbitrary number of images via --count (except for dall-e-3 where it clamps to 1). Large counts and high-resolution sizes can cause long runtimes, high API costs, and large disk usage. This is not an infinite loop, but it is a resource/cost exhaustion vector if invoked with extreme parameters in automated agent runs.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  ap.add_argument("--count", type=int, default=8, help="How many images to generate.")
  ...
  for idx, prompt in enumerate(prompts, start=1):
      res = request_images(...)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add reasonable upper bounds (configurable) for --count and size, or require explicit user confirmation above thresholds. Consider rate limiting/backoff and clearer warnings about cost/disk impact.

This is primarily a reliability/documentation issue, but it can become a safety concern if an agent compensates by fetching external substitutes or inventing policy/templates, potentially leading to inconsistent or unsafe outputs.

  **Remediation:** Increase llm_analysis.max_code_file_chars in your scan policy to include this content in LLM analysis.

  #### [MEDIUM] Packaging script may include unintended files due to broad recursive zipping and arcname choice

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** scripts/package_skill.py

  **Description:** The packager walks the entire skill directory recursively and adds every file it finds to the output archive. This can unintentionally include sensitive or irrelevant artifacts (e.g., .env, editor swap files, build outputs, credentials accidentally placed in the skill folder). Additionally, the archive name is computed relative to the parent of the skill directory, which can embed extra path segments and increase the chance of confusing or unexpected archive layouts when unpacked/consumed by other tooling. While not an active exfiltration vector by itself, it is a tool/data-handling weakness that can lead to accidental exposure when the .skill is shared.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  for file_path in skill_path.rglob('*'):
      if file_path.is_file():
          arcname = file_path.relative_to(skill_path.parent)
          zipf.write(file_path, arcname)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Implement an allowlist of files/directories to include (e.g., SKILL.md, scripts/, assets/, templates/, references/), and explicitly exclude common sensitive patterns (.env, _.key, _.pem, .git/, **pycache**/, \*.swp). Consider setting arcname relative to skill_path (not skill_path.parent) to avoid embedding parent directory structure. Add a pre-packaging report requiring user confirmation when unexpected files are detected.


  **Description:** The SKILL.md states the agent has "direct access to ad platform accounts," which can mislead users into believing the skill can autonomously access/modify real advertising accounts. In the provided package, there are no scripts and no declared tool constraints, and the referenced integration docs are outside the skill directory (blocked as traversal).   ```

  **Description:** The instructions reference multiple files via relative paths that traverse outside the skill package (e.g., ../../tools/...). In many agent runtimes, allowing a skill to read outside its directory can expose unrelated repository content (potentially sensitive) and creates a tool-poisoning surface if those external docs are modified. Your scan already flags these as blocked traversal attempts, indicating the content is attempting cross-boundary access.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  References include: ../../tools/REGISTRY.md and ../../tools/integrations/{google-ads,meta-ads,linkedin-ads,tiktok-ads,ga4,segment}.md (reported as blocked: path traversal attempt).
  ```

  **Remediation:** Bundle required integration guidance inside the skill package (e.g., under references/), or use an allowlisted, sandboxed documentation loader that cannot access arbitrary parent directories. If external docs must be used, pin to a trusted, immutable source and treat as untrusted input (do not follow embedded instructions blindly).

### [OK] slack-gif-creator

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 5
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/slack-gif-creator

  #### [LOW] Manifest omissions reduce enforceability of tool restrictions

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill does not specify allowed-tools or compatibility in the manifest. While optional, this reduces the ability of a host agent framework to enforce least-privilege execution expectations and can lead to over-broad tool access by default.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  allowed-tools: Not specified
  compatibility: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Declare allowed-tools explicitly (likely Python; possibly Read/Write if the agent will read inputs and write outputs) and document expected execution environment/constraints.

  #### [MEDIUM] Potential path clobbering/unsafe file write via user-controlled output_path

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** command_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION
  **Location:** core/gif_builder.py

  **Description:** GIFBuilder.save() writes to an arbitrary filesystem path provided by the caller. In an agent setting, if user input is mapped directly to output_path, this can overwrite existing files (including sensitive locations) or write into unintended directories. While this is not code execution, it is a common agent-safety issue when tools accept untrusted paths without validation or sandboxing.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  def save(self, output_path: str | Path, ...):
      output_path = Path(output_path)
      ...
      imageio.imwrite(output_path, optimized_frames, duration=frame_duration, loop=0)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Constrain output_path to a safe directory (e.g., a dedicated workspace/output folder), reject absolute paths and path traversal (..), and consider prompting for confirmation before overwriting existing files. Optionally implement an allowlist of extensions (e.g., .gif) and use atomic writes (write to temp then rename).

  #### [MEDIUM] Compute/memory exhaustion risk from unbounded frame accumulation and expensive operations

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** resource_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE
  **Location:** core/gif_builder.py

  **Description:** The builder stores all frames in memory as numpy arrays and performs potentially expensive operations (global palette generation via vstack/reshape, per-frame quantization, resizing, and deduplication comparisons). In an agent context, a user could request very large dimensions or very long animations (many frames), causing high RAM/CPU usage and potential denial of service.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  self.frames: list[np.ndarray] = []
  ...
  all_pixels = np.vstack([f.reshape(-1, 3) for f in sample_frames])
  ...
  for frame in self.frames:
      pil_frame = Image.fromarray(frame)
      quantized = pil_frame.quantize(...)
  ...
  for i in range(1, len(self.frames)):
      diff = np.abs(prev_frame - curr_frame)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add guardrails: maximum width/height, maximum frame count, maximum total pixel budget, and early rejection/downsizing. Consider streaming/iterative encoding rather than holding all frames, and add timeouts or progress-based cancellation in agent execution.

  #### [LOW] Missing input validation for numeric parameters (fps, num_colors, dimensions)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** command_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION
  **Location:** core/gif_builder.py

  **Description:** Several parameters (fps, num_colors, width/height) are not range-checked. Extreme or invalid values (e.g., fps=0 leading to division by zero in save(), num_colors outside 8–256, huge dimensions) can cause crashes or resource spikes. This is primarily robustness/availability risk in an agent workflow.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  frame_duration = 1000 / self.fps
  ...
  def optimize_colors(self, num_colors: int = 128, ...):
      ... quantize(colors=num_colors, ...)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Validate ranges at initialization and before processing (fps>=1, reasonable upper bounds; num_colors within Pillow-supported range; width/height within limits). Fail fast with clear errors.

  #### [LOW] Broad exception handling may hide parsing/validation failures

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** command_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION
  **Location:** core/validators.py

  **Description:** validators.validate_gif() uses a bare except when computing duration/fps, which can mask unexpected errors and reduce observability. This is not directly exploitable by itself, but it can hinder detection of malformed inputs and complicate debugging in automated agent runs.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  try:
      duration_ms = img.info.get("duration", 100)
      ...
  except:
      total_duration = None
      fps = None
  ```

  **Remediation:** Catch specific exceptions (e.g., KeyError, ZeroDivisionError, ValueError) and include error details in results when verbose or in a debug mode.
