### [FAIL] webapp-testing

- **Max Severity:** HIGH
- **Findings:** 6
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/webapp-testing

  #### [MEDIUM] Tooling/IO assumptions: examples write to fixed paths that may be unsafe or fail in other environments

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE

  **Description:** Example scripts write artifacts to fixed locations (/mnt/user-data/outputs and /tmp). In some agent runtimes these paths may not exist, may be shared, or may have different permissions, leading to failures or unintended overwrites. While not inherently malicious, it is a tool/IO safety concern for portability and least surprise.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  with open('/mnt/user-data/outputs/console.log', 'w') as f:
  ...
  page.screenshot(path='/mnt/user-data/outputs/static_page.png', full_page=True)
  ...
  page.screenshot(path='/tmp/page_discovery.png', full_page=True)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Write outputs to a user-configurable directory (CLI arg/env var) and ensure directories are created safely. Consider using a per-run unique subdirectory to avoid clobbering.

  #### [LOW] Instruction encourages running scripts before reviewing source (reduces user safety review)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** harmful_content
  **Rule ID:** LLM_HARMFUL_CONTENT
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions advise running helper scripts as black boxes and explicitly discourage reading source first. This is not a direct prompt injection, but it is a risky practice that can increase the chance of executing unexpected behavior without review—especially relevant because the helper uses shell=True and can run arbitrary commands.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  “Always run scripts with --help first… DO NOT read the source until you try running the script first…”
  ```

  **Remediation:** Revise guidance to encourage reviewing scripts (at least the parts that execute commands) before running, and clearly warn that server commands are executed on the host.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools metadata reduces enforceable tool scoping

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest does not specify allowed-tools. In environments that rely on this metadata for policy enforcement or review, omission reduces clarity about intended tool usage (Bash/Python/file writes) and can contribute to capability ambiguity.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Declare allowed-tools explicitly (likely: [Python, Bash, Read, Write] depending on the platform’s tool taxonomy) and document expected file output locations.

  #### [LOW] Referenced files appear missing/inconsistent with package contents

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions/reference section lists files (sync_playwright.py, rendered.py, inspection.py) that are not found. This can cause confusion and may lead users/agents to fetch external replacements or improvise, increasing the risk of unsafe sourcing or tool misuse (though no explicit external fetch is instructed here).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced Files: sync_playwright.py, rendered.py, inspection.py (not found)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove or correct missing references, or include the referenced files in the package. If external sources are intended, explicitly warn that external content is untrusted and must not be executed blindly.

  #### [HIGH] Command injection risk via shell=True for user-supplied server commands

  **Severity:** HIGH
  **Category:** command_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION
  **Location:** scripts/with_server.py

  **Description:** The helper script starts servers using subprocess.Popen(..., shell=True) with the --server argument value. Because --server is user-controlled input, this enables arbitrary shell command execution (e.g., command chaining, redirection). While the skill’s purpose is to run local dev servers, using shell=True materially increases the risk of command injection if untrusted input is ever passed through (e.g., from a user request, copied command, or higher-level agent automation).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  process = subprocess.Popen(
      server['cmd'],
      shell=True,
      stdout=subprocess.PIPE,
      stderr=subprocess.PIPE
  )
  ```

  **Remediation:** Avoid shell=True. Accept server commands as an argv list (e.g., --server-cmd and --server-args) and invoke subprocess.Popen([...], shell=False). If shell features are required, strictly validate/allowlist commands, reject metacharacters (;, &&, |, >, $(), backticks), and clearly document that --server must never be derived from untrusted input.

  #### [MEDIUM] Potential availability impact from unbounded server output buffering (stdout/stderr PIPE)

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** resource_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE
  **Location:** scripts/with_server.py

  **Description:** Server processes are started with stdout/stderr captured to subprocess.PIPE but the script never reads from these pipes. If a server writes enough output, the OS pipe buffer can fill and block the server process, causing hangs/timeouts and disrupting availability of the testing workflow.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  stdout=subprocess.PIPE,
  stderr=subprocess.PIPE
  # (no subsequent reads from process.stdout/process.stderr)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Do not pipe output unless you consume it. Options: inherit parent stdout/stderr (stdout=None, stderr=None), redirect to files, or spawn reader threads to continuously drain pipes.

While this may be legitimate for the tool, it is a sensitive operation that can expose session material if mishandled (e.g., logs, accidental sharing, or an agent reading/exporting cookie stores). The skill does not provide safeguards or warnings about handling authentication artifacts.


### [OK] mcporter

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 4
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/mcporter

  #### [MEDIUM] Indirect prompt-injection / transitive trust risk through remote MCP servers and fetched URLs

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill encourages calling remote MCP endpoints and passing URLs to tools (e.g., `mcp.fetch url:https://example.com`). If the agent consumes tool outputs (schemas, descriptions, or fetched content) as instructions, a malicious MCP server or fetched webpage could embed instruction-manipulation content that influences subsequent agent actions (classic indirect prompt injection). The skill documentation does not include guidance to treat remote tool outputs/content as untrusted data.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Remote call example: `mcporter call https://api.example.com/mcp.fetch url:https://example.com` and schema listing: `mcporter list <server> --schema`.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit guidance: treat all remote server responses, schemas, and fetched webpage content as untrusted data; never execute instructions found in tool output; apply output filtering/validation; require user confirmation before acting on remote-provided directives. Consider domain allowlisting and TLS/identity verification recommendations.

  #### [MEDIUM] Capability inflation / broad tool-calling surface without declared tool restrictions

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill is positioned as a general-purpose interface to list/configure/authenticate and call arbitrary MCP servers/tools over HTTP and stdio, including ad-hoc server execution (e.g., running a local command via stdio transport). This significantly expands the agent’s effective capability surface (network access, local process execution, authentication flows) but the manifest does not specify `allowed-tools` or any guardrails/constraints. While `allowed-tools` is optional, the combination of broad operational scope + lack of explicit restrictions increases the risk of unintended activation and overbroad use in contexts where a narrower skill would be expected.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Examples include: `mcporter call https://api.example.com/mcp.fetch url:https://example.com` and `mcporter call --stdio "bun run ./server.ts" scrape url=https://example.com` plus auth/config/daemon/codegen features.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit `allowed-tools` in the manifest aligned to intended use (e.g., Bash if CLI execution is required; otherwise restrict). Document safety boundaries: require user confirmation before (1) calling remote URLs, (2) running stdio commands, (3) performing auth/login, and (4) starting daemons. Consider adding a policy section describing permitted servers/domains and how secrets are handled.

  #### [MEDIUM] Potential unauthorized tool use via stdio command execution pattern

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions explicitly demonstrate invoking an ad-hoc stdio server by running a local command (`bun run ./server.ts`). In an agent setting, this pattern can translate into arbitrary command execution if user-controlled strings are passed through to the CLI invocation, or if the agent is encouraged to run such commands without confirmation. Even though no scripts are included here, the skill’s documented usage encourages a tool-execution pathway that can be abused for running untrusted local code.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  `mcporter call --stdio "bun run ./server.ts" scrape url=https://example.com`
  ```

  **Remediation:** Require explicit user confirmation and/or an allowlist for stdio commands. Recommend using fixed, vetted server entrypoints rather than arbitrary command strings. Add guidance to never interpolate untrusted input into the stdio command and to avoid running code from untrusted repositories.

  #### [LOW] Unreferenced Python scripts present in package inventory (potential hidden functionality)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** Static inventory indicates four Python files exist (`core/gif_builder.py`, `core/frame_composer.py`, `core/validators.py`, `core/easing.py`) but they are not referenced in the provided SKILL.md and were not included for review. Unreferenced executables can indicate hidden or leftover functionality and should be reviewed to ensure they do not introduce unexpected behaviors (e.g., file access, network calls, or code execution).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Pre-scan inventory: unreferenced_scripts: ['core/gif_builder.py', 'core/frame_composer.py', 'core/validators.py', 'core/easing.py']
  ```

  **Remediation:** Include these files in the skill’s documented behavior or remove them. Perform code review for network access, filesystem traversal, subprocess usage, dynamic execution (eval/exec), and secret handling. Add tests/CI checks to prevent shipping unreferenced executables.

### [OK] loom-transcript

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 5
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/loom-transcript

  #### [LOW] Inconsistency and suspicious inventory: referenced files missing and unreferenced Python scripts present

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE

  **Description:** The instructions reference files (a.py, step.py) that are not found, while the pre-scan inventory reports multiple unreferenced Python scripts under core/ (gif_builder.py, frame_composer.py, validators.py, easing.py). This mismatch can indicate packaging errors or hidden/unused capabilities that are not described in the manifest/instructions, reducing auditability and potentially enabling capability inflation or unexpected behavior if those scripts are invoked elsewhere.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced but missing: a.py, step.py. Unreferenced scripts: core/gif_builder.py, core/frame_composer.py, core/validators.py, core/easing.py.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove stale references or include the referenced files. Document and justify any included scripts, or remove unused code from the package. Ensure SKILL.md accurately reflects the actual executable components.

  #### [MEDIUM] Potential command injection risk if video ID/URLs are interpolated into shell commands without strict validation

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** command_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The workflow requires extracting a video ID from user-provided input ($ARGUMENTS) and substituting it into JSON payloads and later fetching URLs returned by the API using curl. If an implementation naively interpolates the extracted ID or returned URLs into shell commands (e.g., unquoted variables, string concatenation), it can enable shell injection. The instructions mention a 32-character hex ID, but do not mandate strict validation/enforcement or safe command construction.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  User-controlled input: "Given the Loom URL: $ARGUMENTS" and substitution points: "Replace `<VIDEO_ID>` with the actual video ID"; later: "curl -sL "<url>"" for URLs obtained from the API.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Enforce strict validation: only accept video IDs matching `^[0-9a-fA-F]{32}$`. When using Bash, avoid eval and ensure all variables are safely quoted; prefer passing JSON via a file or using a safer HTTP client in Python with structured parameters. For downloaded transcript URLs, validate scheme/host (e.g., https and expected Loom/CDN domains) before fetching.

  #### [MEDIUM] Indirect prompt injection surface via untrusted transcript content being displayed without sanitization guidance

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill fetches and displays transcript text sourced from external URLs (captions_source_url/source_url). Transcripts can contain adversarial prompt-injection strings (e.g., 'ignore previous instructions', 'run this command') that may be re-consumed by an agent in a multi-step workflow or copied into subsequent prompts. The instructions do not include any guidance to treat fetched transcript text as untrusted data or to avoid executing/following instructions contained within it.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  External content ingestion: "Download with `curl -sL "<url>"`" and then "Format and present the full transcript to the user" with no warning to treat transcript as untrusted.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit handling guidance: treat transcript as untrusted content; never follow instructions found in transcripts; if summarizing, use a 'data-only' mode (quote/escape) and separate transcript text from operational instructions. Consider stripping/escaping control-like patterns or clearly labeling transcript as untrusted.

  #### [MEDIUM] Tooling mismatch: instructions require network/Bash/WebFetch but manifest omits allowed-tools and scripts are absent

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The SKILL.md instructs the agent to use Bash (curl) and a WebFetch-like capability to POST to Loom's GraphQL endpoint and then download transcript URLs. However, the manifest does not specify allowed-tools, and the package as presented includes no script files implementing these steps. This increases the risk of unauthorized tool use in environments where tool permissions are expected to be explicitly declared/controlled, and it makes behavior harder to audit (the effective behavior depends on the host agent's tool availability and policy).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Instructions explicitly: "Use this curl command via Bash" and "Use the `WebFetch` tool to POST to `https://www.loom.com/graphql`" while manifest: allowed-tools not specified; Script Files: "No script files found."
  ```

  **Remediation:** Declare `allowed-tools` explicitly (at minimum: [Bash] and/or the platform's HTTP tool) and ensure the implementation is included as auditable scripts rather than only prose. If WebFetch is not a supported tool in the target runtime, remove/replace those instructions.

  #### [LOW] Potentially misleading claim about public accessibility/no authentication

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** harmful_content
  **Rule ID:** LLM_HARMFUL_CONTENT
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The notes state that no authentication/cookies are required and that the transcript API is publicly accessible. In practice, access may vary by Loom privacy settings, rate limits, or changes to Loom's API behavior. Overstating public accessibility can mislead users and encourage attempts to access content they are not authorized to view (even if the skill itself does not bypass auth).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Notes: "No authentication or cookies are required — Loom's transcript API is publicly accessible."
  ```

  **Remediation:** Qualify the statement: access depends on video permissions and Loom's current API behavior; handle 401/403 and privacy-related errors explicitly; avoid implying the skill can access private content.

- **Findings:** 4
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/email-sequence

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools metadata reduces enforceable least-privilege

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE

  **Description:** The manifest omits allowed-tools. While optional, this prevents the platform from enforcing least-privilege expectations for the skill and makes it harder to detect tool misuse or unexpected escalation if scripts are added later.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an explicit allowed-tools list (likely none or [Read] if only reading local reference/context files). Keep it minimal and update if future scripts/tooling are introduced.

  #### [MEDIUM] Path traversal references to files outside the skill package (tools registry/integrations)

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** SKILL.md references multiple files via relative paths that traverse upward (e.g., ../../tools/...). In many skill runners, resolving these paths would allow the skill to read content outside its own package boundary (tool registries, integration guides, or other repo files). Even if the current environment blocks these reads (as indicated), the skill design still attempts cross-boundary access and could become exploitable in a different host configuration or if path validation is bypassed. This is a tool-boundary / unauthorized file access risk and can also enable indirect prompt injection if external instructions are pulled from outside the package.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  References include: ../../tools/REGISTRY.md and ../../tools/integrations/{sendgrid,mailchimp,resend,customer-io,kit}.md (flagged as "blocked: path traversal attempt").
  ```

  **Remediation:** Constrain references to files within the skill directory (no ../ segments). If cross-repo docs are required, vendor/copy the needed content into a local references/ directory. Enforce canonical-path checks in the loader (reject any path that escapes the skill root) and treat any out-of-package content as untrusted with explicit allowlists.

  #### [LOW] Unreferenced Python scripts present in package inventory (capability ambiguity)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** Static inventory reports four Python files (gif_builder.py, frame_composer.py, validators.py, easing.py) that are not referenced by SKILL.md. Unreferenced executables can indicate leftover code, hidden functionality, or future activation paths not documented in the manifest/instructions. No code was provided here to assess for exfiltration/injection, but their presence increases uncertainty and can be used for capability inflation or stealthy behavior if the runtime auto-discovers scripts.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Pre-Scan Context: unreferenced_scripts: ['core/gif_builder.py', 'core/frame_composer.py', 'core/validators.py', 'core/easing.py']
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove unused scripts from the package, or document their purpose and invocation path in SKILL.md. Ensure the runtime only executes explicitly referenced/allowed entrypoints. Perform code review of these files for network access, filesystem traversal, exec/eval, and secret handling.

### [FAIL] ai-elements

- **Max Severity:** CRITICAL
- **Findings:** 51

  #### [HIGH] Critically low analyzability score

  **Severity:** HIGH
  **Category:** policy_violation
  **Rule ID:** LOW_ANALYZABILITY

  **Description:** Only 41% of skill content could be analyzed. 80 of 130 files are opaque to the scanner. The safety assessment has low confidence.

  **Remediation:** Replace opaque files (binaries, encrypted content) with inspectable source code to improve scan confidence.

  #### [MEDIUM] Supply-chain / indirect instruction risk via remote code execution patterns (npx @latest)

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION

  **Description:** Instructions recommend running `npx ai-elements@latest`, which fetches and executes remote package code at install time. This is a common supply-chain risk vector: the executed code can change over time, and compromise of the package or dependency chain could lead to malicious execution on developer machines/CI.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```bash
  npx ai-elements@latest
  ```

  **Remediation:** Recommend pinned versions (e.g., `npx ai-elements@<known-good-version>`), document integrity verification (lockfiles, npm audit), and advise running in a controlled environment (CI sandbox) when possible.

  #### [LOW] External links and remote media in examples create transitive trust and tracking surface

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION

  **Description:** Multiple examples reference external URLs (npm, GitHub issues, Unsplash images, Vercel blob storage, third-party docs). While typical for documentation, these links can become a transitive trust vector (content changes, tracking, or malicious replacement) if an agent is instructed to fetch/interpret them or if developers blindly follow them.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Examples include https://www.npmjs.com/package/ai-elements, https://github.com/vercel/ai-elements/issues, Unsplash image URLs, and a remote MP3 URL.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Where possible, prefer stable, official sources; warn users not to execute instructions from fetched web content without review; consider bundling critical docs locally in `references/`.

  #### [LOW] Over-broad trigger keywords may cause unintended activation (capability/activation inflation)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse

  **Description:** The description includes a long list of generic trigger phrases (e.g., "chat UI", "message component", "prompt input"). This can increase accidental invocation in unrelated contexts, which is a mild form of discovery/activation abuse even if the skill itself is benign.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Triggers on "ai-elements", "AI chat components", "chat UI", "message component", "prompt input", "shadcn ai"...
  ```

  **Remediation:** Narrow triggers to distinctive, high-signal phrases (e.g., "ai-elements" and "Vercel AI Elements") and avoid generic UI terms.

  #### [MEDIUM] Example code transmits audio to third-party API and uses environment-based API key (potential data exposure)

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** scripts/speech-input.tsx

  **Description:** The `speech-input.tsx` example posts recorded audio to OpenAI's transcription endpoint and attaches an API key from an environment variable. While this is presented as a fallback example, it creates a clear data-flow pattern (user audio -> external service) that can lead to privacy issues if adopted without consent/notice, and it encourages handling secrets in client-adjacent code paths. Additionally, using `NEXT_PUBLIC_OPENAI_API_KEY` suggests a public-exposed key in Next.js, which is unsafe if used client-side.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  fetch("https://api.openai.com/v1/audio/transcriptions", { headers: { Authorization: `Bearer ${process.env.NEXT_PUBLIC_OPENAI_API_KEY}` }, method: "POST" })
  ```

  **Remediation:** Ensure transcription requests are made server-side only; never use `NEXT_PUBLIC_*` for secrets. Add explicit user consent/notice for audio upload, minimize data retention, and document secure secret management (server env vars, API routes).

  #### [MEDIUM] Outbound network request primitives in JavaScript/TypeScript

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** DATA_EXFIL_JS_NETWORK
  **Location:** scripts/speech-input.tsx:16

  **Description:** Pattern detected: fetch(

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  const response = await fetch(
  ```

  **Remediation:** Ensure network operations are necessary and document allowed destinations

  **Description:** The manifest metadata shows signs of being "ported" and contains an anomalous lastUpdated value ("12026-02-04"), while key fields like compatibility and allowed-tools are unspecified. These issues are not direct exploits, but they can contribute to capability/activation ambiguity during skill discovery and review, and make it harder to enforce tool restrictions in agent runtimes.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  - compatibility: Not specified
  - allowed-tools: Not specified
  - additional metadata: {... 'lastUpdated': '12026-02-04', 'provenance': 'ported'}
  ```

  **Remediation:** Fix metadata: provide a valid lastUpdated date, specify compatibility, and (optionally) declare allowed-tools to match intended usage (likely none, or Read/Grep/Glob if later adding scripts). Ensure the description accurately reflects any future scripts or external interactions.

  #### [LOW] Referenced file missing (most.py) may cause unexpected tool behavior or fallback to untrusted sources

  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The SKILL.md references a file named "most.py", but it is not present in the provided package content. Missing referenced artifacts can lead to brittle behavior (skill instructions that cannot be followed as written) and, in some agent runtimes, may encourage operators/agents to fetch substitutes from external sources or to execute similarly-named files found elsewhere on disk—creating an avoidable tool/behavioral risk. While no explicit malicious behavior is present, the inconsistency should be corrected to prevent accidental tool misuse or shadowing.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced Files: Files referenced in instructions: most.py

  **Referenced File: most.py** (not found)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Either (1) remove the reference to most.py from SKILL.md if not required, or (2) include the intended most.py in the skill package and document exactly how it is used. If the file is meant to be user-provided, explicitly mark it as untrusted input and add validation/safety guidance (e.g., do not execute arbitrary code from it).

### [OK] nextjs-cache-components

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 7
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/nextjs-cache-components

  #### [MEDIUM] Auto-activation / proactive behavior may cause unintended skill invocation

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill declares it will "activate automatically" when it detects `cacheComponents: true` in a Next.js project and then "automatically apply these patterns". While not inherently malicious, this is a form of capability/activation inflation that can increase the chance the skill is invoked outside explicit user intent, potentially leading to overreach (e.g., unsolicited code changes or recommendations) in agent workflows.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  > **Auto-activation**: This skill activates automatically in projects with `cacheComponents: true`...
  ...
  When `cacheComponents: true` is detected in the project, **automatically apply these patterns**
  ```

  **Remediation:** Require explicit user confirmation before applying changes or recommendations; scope activation to explicit user requests (e.g., only when the user asks about caching/PPR); document clear activation criteria and safe defaults.

  #### [LOW] Tooling implied in instructions but allowed-tools is unspecified

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest omits `allowed-tools` while the instructions include a Bash command (`grep -r ...`). Missing `allowed-tools` is allowed per spec, but it reduces clarity about intended tool usage and can complicate enforcement of least privilege.

  **Code Snippet:**
  YAML Manifest Details: allowed-tools: Not specified

  Instructions include:

  ```bash
  grep -r "cacheComponents" next.config.* 2>/dev/null
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an explicit `allowed-tools` list (e.g., `[Read, Grep, Glob]` if only scanning is needed; include `Bash` only if required). Prefer non-shell file inspection via agent file tools when possible.

  #### [LOW] Use of Bash grep in instructions could be unsafe if generalized to user-controlled paths

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The provided `grep -r` example is relatively constrained (`next.config.*`), but it normalizes using shell commands for project detection. In some agent runtimes, similar patterns can drift into broader recursive searches or be adapted with user-controlled input, increasing risk of unintended file traversal or command misuse.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```bash
  # Check next.config.ts or next.config.js for cacheComponents
  grep -r "cacheComponents" next.config.* 2>/dev/null
  ```

  **Remediation:** Keep detection constrained to specific filenames; avoid `-r` unless necessary; do not interpolate user input into shell commands; prefer safe file reads and string search via built-in tools.

  #### [LOW] Broken/missing referenced files reduce auditability and may encourage unsafe fallback behaviors

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions reference multiple internal files (REFERENCE/PATTERNS/TROUBLESHOOTING) but many are missing or were not analyzable due to budget. This is not a direct exploit by itself, but it reduces transparency and can lead to agents seeking external resources or improvising, which increases the chance of indirect prompt injection in real use if the agent fetches replacements from the web.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced files include multiple "not found" entries (e.g., templates/TROUBLESHOOTING.md, templates/PATTERNS.md, templates/REFERENCE.md, assets/*, type.py) and several "skipped: exceeds budget" (references/*).
  ```

  **Remediation:** Ensure referenced files exist within the package and are included in review; avoid instructing the agent to fetch external docs as a fallback; if external links are necessary, explicitly warn to treat them as untrusted and not to follow embedded instructions.

  ```
  Referenced: `assets/ideas-by-category.md` (not found), `templates/ideas-by-category.md` (not found), `references/ideas-by-category.md` (skipped: exceeds budget)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Ensure referenced files exist and are included in the package; keep referenced documents reasonably sized for review; avoid instructing the agent to substitute external sources if a reference is missing; add checks to fail closed when references are unavailable.

  #### [LOW] Untrusted local context file is auto-consumed without validation (possible indirect prompt injection)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill instructs the agent to automatically read a local context file (e.g., `.agents/product-marketing-context.md` or `.claude/product-marketing-context.md`) if it exists and to use it as authoritative context. While local files are not inherently unsafe, these paths are outside the skill package and may be user- or repo-controlled. If an attacker can modify that file (e.g., via a compromised repo, malicious PR, or another tool), they can inject instructions that manipulate the agent’s behavior (e.g., add hidden directives, data collection requests, or tool-use guidance). This is a classic transitive-trust/indirect prompt injection risk.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "If `.agents/product-marketing-context.md` exists (or `.claude/product-marketing-context.md` in older setups), read it before asking questions. Use that context..."
  ```

  **Remediation:** Treat these files as untrusted input: (1) clearly scope what may be extracted (facts only, no instructions), (2) ignore any imperative language in the file, (3) optionally require user confirmation before using external context files, and (4) consider restricting to a skill-bundled context file or a signed/verified source.

  #### [INFO] 'references/ideas-by-category.md' excluded from LLM analysis (15,216 chars)

  **Severity:** INFO
  **Category:** policy_violation
  **Rule ID:** LLM_CONTEXT_BUDGET_EXCEEDED
  **Location:** references/ideas-by-category.md

  **Description:** file size (15,216 chars) exceeds per-file limit (8,000)

  **Remediation:** Increase llm_analysis.max_referenced_file_chars in your scan policy to include this content in LLM analysis.

  #### [HIGH] Path traversal references to files outside the skill package

  **Severity:** HIGH
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE

  **Description:** The skill references files using relative paths that traverse outside the skill directory (e.g., "../../tools/..."), which can enable unauthorized access to host/agent files if an agent follows these references. Even if the current loader blocked these paths, their presence is a strong indicator of an attempt to reach external resources (tooling registry/integration docs) beyond the skill boundary.


  **Remediation:** Remove all "../"-based references. Restrict file access to the skill package root via canonicalization checks (resolve realpath and enforce prefix). If external documentation is required, vendor a safe copy inside the package and reference it with a package-relative path.

  #### [MEDIUM] Capability inflation / ambiguous scope via extensive referenced resources without visible instructions

  #### [MEDIUM] Potential capability inflation via broad operational guidance (git history, running tests) without explicit constraints

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE

  **Description:** The skill provides broad operational steps (e.g., running test runners, inspecting git history) that can expand the agent’s effective capabilities in practice. Without explicit constraints (tool allowlist, scope limits, confirmation gates), this can be used as a discovery/activation vector to justify wider actions than necessary for “reproduce with a failing test.”

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Examples include: `git log --oneline -10 -- <path>` and instructions to run the project's test runner.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit scope limits: only operate within the repository root, avoid accessing home directory/secrets, require confirmation before running commands, and specify the minimal commands expected. Include a safety note to avoid reading credential files or environment secrets.

  #### [MEDIUM] Instruction encourages shell redirection and log creation without explicit tool/permission boundaries

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE

  **Description:** The instructions recommend redirecting build output to a log file (`command > build.log 2>&1`). In an agentic setting, this implies use of Bash and file writes. Because `allowed-tools` is not declared, there is no explicit boundary preventing broader tool use, and the guidance could lead to unintended file creation/overwrites in the working directory. This is not inherently malicious, but it is a tool-safety concern in constrained environments.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  "Always redirect build output": `command > build.log 2>&1`
  ```

  **Remediation:** Declare `allowed-tools` explicitly. If file writes are intended, document safe paths (e.g., a dedicated logs/ directory), avoid overwriting (e.g., `>>` with timestamped filenames), and require user confirmation before running commands that write files.

  #### [LOW] Referenced files appear inconsistent/missing, increasing risk of unintended trust in external or user-provided substitutes

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION

  **Description:** The instructions/reference list includes files (signals.py, entry.py, Step.py, the.py) that are not found. In practice, an agent might search for or fetch similarly named files from outside the package or accept user-provided replacements, which can become an indirect prompt-injection vector if those external contents are treated as authoritative instructions.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced files: signals.py, entry.py, Step.py, the.py (all not found)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove nonexistent references or include the intended internal files. Add guidance: do not fetch external files/URLs to satisfy missing references; treat any user-provided documents as untrusted and do not execute instructions from them.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools and compatibility metadata reduces enforceable tool-scope clarity

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE

  **Description:** The manifest omits `allowed-tools` and `compatibility`. While optional, this reduces the ability to enforce least-privilege tool usage and to reason about whether the skill is intended to run in environments with different tool access. This is primarily a governance/clarity issue rather than an exploit by itself.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  YAML Manifest Details:
  - compatibility: Not specified
  - allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add `allowed-tools` reflecting the minimum required (likely [Read, Grep, Glob] and optionally [Bash, Write] only if truly needed). Specify `compatibility` targets. Keep the description aligned with the declared tool scope.

### [OK] bear-notes

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 4
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/bear-notes

  #### [MEDIUM] Sensitive token handling guidance may lead to credential exposure

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill instructs users to store a Bear API token in a predictable plaintext location ("~/.config/grizzly/token") and to pass it via "--token-file". While this is a common pattern for CLIs, it increases the risk of accidental exposure (e.g., other tools/processes reading the file, backups, mis-set file permissions, or the agent being prompted to read and reveal the token). The skill does not provide guidance on file permissions (chmod), redaction, or avoiding printing token contents.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Save it: `echo "YOUR_TOKEN" > ~/.config/grizzly/token`
  ...
  `grizzly add-text ... --token-file ~/.config/grizzly/token`
  `grizzly tags ... --token-file ~/.config/grizzly/token`
  ```

  **Remediation:** Recommend secure storage and permissions (e.g., `umask 077` and `chmod 600 ~/.config/grizzly/token`), warn never to paste token into chats/logs, and advise the agent to redact tokens if reading config. Consider using macOS Keychain integration if supported by grizzly.

  #### [MEDIUM] Local configuration file trust can enable indirect instruction manipulation

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill documents that grizzly reads configuration from ".grizzly.toml" in the current directory (higher priority than the user config). In agentic contexts, operating inside an untrusted repository could allow a malicious project to influence behavior (e.g., changing callback_url, token_file path, or timeouts) without the user noticing. This is a form of indirect prompt/instruction manipulation via external (project) data that the skill implicitly trusts.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Grizzly reads config from (in priority order):
  ...
  3. `.grizzly.toml` in current directory
  ...
  Example config includes `callback_url = "http://127.0.0.1:42123/success"`
  ```

  **Remediation:** Advise users/agent to prefer explicit CLI flags for sensitive settings, or to ignore/disable project-local config when operating in untrusted directories. Add a warning to inspect `.grizzly.toml` before running commands and to restrict callback URLs to localhost.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools metadata reduces enforceable tool scoping

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest does not specify `allowed-tools`. While optional, omission reduces the ability to constrain the agent’s tool use (e.g., limiting to Bash only) and makes it harder to detect tool-scope violations during execution.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  YAML Manifest Details: allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an `allowed-tools` list aligned to intended usage (likely `[Bash]` and possibly `[Read]` if needed for local config inspection).

  #### [LOW] Referenced file missing (Bear.py) may indicate packaging inconsistency or hidden functionality elsewhere

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions reference a file (Bear.py) that is not present in the provided referenced file content. This can indicate incomplete packaging, documentation drift, or that functionality is implemented elsewhere (e.g., in the larger repository) and not captured in the skill description, complicating security review.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced Files: Files referenced in instructions: Bear.py
  Referenced File: Bear.py (not found)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove the reference if obsolete, or include the file and review it for unsafe behaviors (networking, token access, command execution). Ensure documentation matches shipped artifacts.

### [FAIL] trello-api

- **Max Severity:** CRITICAL
- **Findings:** 17
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/trello-api

  #### [MEDIUM] Shell command injection risk via templated curl commands with user-supplied IDs/text

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** command_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill instructs users/agent to run curl commands containing unquoted, templated path segments and parameters (e.g., {boardId}, {listId}, {cardId}, {newListId}) and free-form text fields (e.g., comment text). If an agent naively substitutes user-provided values into these command strings and executes them via Bash, an attacker could inject shell metacharacters (e.g., `;`, `$(...)`, backticks) to execute arbitrary commands on the host. This is a common risk when skills provide copy/paste shell templates intended to be parameterized from untrusted input.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Examples include:
  - `curl -s "https://api.trello.com/1/boards/{boardId}/lists?key=$TRELLO_API_KEY&token=$TRELLO_TOKEN" ...`
  - `curl -s -X PUT "https://api.trello.com/1/cards/{cardId}?key=$TRELLO_API_KEY&token=$TRELLO_TOKEN" -d "idList={newListId}"`
  - `curl -s -X POST "https://api.trello.com/1/cards/{cardId}/actions/comments?..." -d "text=Your comment here"`
  ```

  **Remediation:** If this skill is meant to be executed by an agent:

- Avoid constructing shell commands via string interpolation; prefer a dedicated HTTP client tool/library or a wrapper script that passes arguments as an array (no shell).
- If Bash must be used, strictly validate IDs against expected patterns (e.g., Trello IDs are typically alphanumeric) and reject any value containing shell metacharacters/whitespace.
- Quote defensively and use `--data-urlencode` for user text (comments/desc) to avoid breaking argument boundaries.
- Consider providing a safe helper script that accepts parameters and never evals/execs arbitrary strings.

  #### [MEDIUM] Credential exposure risk by placing Trello API key/token in URL query strings

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions place `key` and `token` in the request URL query string. Query strings are commonly captured in shell history, process listings, logs, proxies, and monitoring tools. While Trello’s API supports this pattern, it increases the chance of accidental credential exposure on the local machine or in shared logs.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  `curl -s "https://api.trello.com/1/members/me/boards?key=$TRELLO_API_KEY&token=$TRELLO_TOKEN" ...`
  ```

  **Remediation:** - Prefer sending credentials in headers if supported, or at minimum use `--config` files with restricted permissions and avoid echoing commands.

- Document operational hygiene: disable/avoid shell history for these commands, avoid sharing terminal output, and ensure logs do not capture full URLs.
- Consider using short-lived tokens and least-privilege where possible.

  #### [LOW] External link in setup could be abused for indirect instruction manipulation if agent is allowed to browse

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The setup section includes a URL to obtain an API key. If an agent is configured to browse/fetch web content and to follow instructions found on external pages, this creates an indirect prompt-injection surface (external content could include malicious instructions). The current skill text does not explicitly instruct the agent to fetch and follow the page content, but the presence of the link is a potential transitive-trust footgun in autonomous settings.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  `Get your API key: https://trello.com/app-key`
  ```

  **Remediation:** - Clarify that the link is for the human user only and that the agent must not ingest/execute instructions from external pages.

- If browsing is supported, implement a policy: treat fetched web content as untrusted data; never execute embedded commands from it.

  #### [LOW] Referenced file mismatch (the.py) suggests packaging inconsistency and potential confusion/abuse

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions/reference list mentions `the.py`, but no script files are present and the referenced file is not found. While not directly malicious, missing/phantom references can be used to mislead reviewers about what code exists or to create ambiguity that later enables a swapped-in script (supply-chain style update) without clear review.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced Files: `the.py (not found)`; Script Files: `No script files found.`
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove the reference if unintended, or include the file and ensure it is reviewed. Add versioning/provenance metadata and a changelog to reduce the risk of silent capability changes.

  #### [LOW] Tooling assumptions and missing allowed-tools declaration may lead to unsafe execution defaults

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest omits `allowed-tools` while the skill clearly expects Bash execution (curl/jq). In some agent runtimes, missing tool declarations can lead to overly permissive defaults or unclear enforcement, increasing the chance the agent uses additional tools (e.g., file reads) beyond what is intended.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Manifest: `allowed-tools: Not specified` while instructions are entirely Bash/curl-based.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Explicitly declare `allowed-tools` (e.g., `[Bash]`) and document that no file access is required. If the agent should never read local files or environment beyond the two variables, state that explicitly.

  #### [CRITICAL] Dangerous data flow in command pipeline

  **Severity:** CRITICAL
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** PIPELINE_TAINT_FLOW
  **Location:** SKILL.md:1

  **Description:** Pipeline reads sensitive data and sends it over the network: `curl -s "https://api.trello.com/1/members/me/boards?key=$TRELLO_API_KEY&token=$TRELLO_TOKEN" | jq '.[] | {name, id}'`. This is likely data exfiltration.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  curl -s "https://api.trello.com/1/members/me/boards?key=$TRELLO_API_KEY&token=$TRELLO_TOKEN" | jq '.[] | {name, id}'
  ```

  **Remediation:** Review the command pipeline. Avoid piping sensitive data to network commands or shell execution.

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 5
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/eightctl

  #### [MEDIUM] File extension does not match actual content type

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** obfuscation
  **Rule ID:** FILE_MAGIC_MISMATCH
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** File 'SKILL.md' extension (.md) suggests one format but Magika detected a different text format: YAML source (yaml). This may indicate content obfuscation or a misnamed file.

  **Remediation:** Rename the file to match its actual content type, or remove it if it appears malicious.

  #### [LOW] Credentials handling guidance may increase exposure risk (env vars and plaintext config)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions indicate authentication via environment variables (`EIGHTCTL_EMAIL`, `EIGHTCTL_PASSWORD`) and a config file at `~/.config/eightctl/config.yaml`. While common for CLIs, this can lead to accidental credential exposure (shell history, process environment inspection, overly permissive file permissions, backups). No exfiltration is shown (no scripts), but the pattern is sensitive.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Auth
  - Config: `~/.config/eightctl/config.yaml`
  - Env: `EIGHTCTL_EMAIL`, `EIGHTCTL_PASSWORD`
  ```

  **Remediation:** Recommend secure storage practices: prefer OS keychain/credential manager if supported; ensure config file permissions (e.g., chmod 600); avoid exporting passwords in shared shells; document how to rotate/revoke credentials. If tokens are supported, prefer scoped tokens over passwords.

  #### [LOW] Unofficial API usage note without safety guardrails could lead to account/device issues

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** harmful_content
  **Rule ID:** LLM_HARMFUL_CONTENT
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill notes the API is unofficial and rate-limited and advises avoiding repeated logins. Without additional guardrails, users/agents may still automate actions that could trigger rate limits or account lockouts, potentially causing service disruption or unexpected device behavior. This is a minor safety/documentation concern rather than a direct exploit.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Notes
  - API is unofficial and rate-limited; avoid repeated logins.
  - Confirm before changing temperature or alarms.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit guidance to implement backoff/retry limits, require user confirmation for state-changing actions, and avoid continuous polling. Document potential consequences (rate limiting, lockouts) and safe operational limits.

  #### [LOW] Potential capability/activation inflation via broad CLI control description without implementation details

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill describes controlling IoT devices (Eight Sleep pods) and includes authentication methods, but no scripts are present. This mismatch can confuse users/agents about what is actually implemented and may encourage unsafe assumptions about capabilities or automation. This is not evidence of malicious behavior, but it is a consistency/expectation risk.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Description: "Control Eight Sleep pods via CLI..."; Script Files: "No script files found."
  ```

  **Remediation:** Clarify in SKILL.md whether this skill is documentation-only, requires an external binary/package, or expects the user to install `eightctl` separately. If external installation is required, document trusted installation sources and integrity checks.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools declaration limits enforcement of tool restrictions

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill manifest does not specify `allowed-tools`. While optional, the absence of explicit tool restrictions reduces the ability to enforce least-privilege expectations (e.g., preventing unintended file reads/writes or command execution if scripts are later added).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  YAML Manifest Details: allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an `allowed-tools` list reflecting the minimum required capabilities (e.g., if this is purely documentation/CLI usage guidance, consider restricting to no tools or only those needed). Reassess if scripts are added later.

### [FAIL] issue-analysis

- **Max Severity:** HIGH
- **Findings:** 4
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/issue-analysis

  #### [HIGH] Untrusted external content ingestion (images/videos/docs) enables indirect prompt injection and unsafe instruction following

  **Severity:** HIGH
  **Category:** prompt_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill mandates fetching and analyzing all visual/media content and external documents linked from issue descriptions/comments (e.g., arbitrary image URLs, Loom videos, Notion/Confluence/Google Docs). This creates a transitive-trust pipeline where attacker-controlled issue content can supply malicious instructions embedded in images (screenshots), transcripts, or documents. Because the workflow requires comprehensive fetching and summarization, the agent may inadvertently follow embedded instructions (e.g., “run this command”, “exfiltrate this file”, “change settings”) or treat them as authoritative context. No guardrails are specified to treat fetched content as untrusted or to prohibit executing instructions found in fetched artifacts.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  “You MUST scan and fetch all visual content from BOTH the issue description AND all comments.”
  “For EACH image found… Download using curl… View the downloaded file to analyze it”
  “If links to documentation platforms are present… fetch content if tools are available”
  “For EACH video… Use a transcript-fetching skill or tool if available”
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit instructions: treat all fetched content (images, transcripts, docs) as untrusted data; never execute commands or follow procedural instructions found within it. Require user confirmation before acting on any instruction derived from external artifacts. Consider allowlisting domains for downloads and document sources, and implement content sanitization/redaction steps.

  #### [MEDIUM] Unsafe tool usage pattern: arbitrary URL downloads via curl to fixed path in /tmp

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions direct the agent to download every discovered image URL using curl with redirects enabled and save to a fixed filename (/tmp/image.png). This can be abused to (1) overwrite the same file repeatedly (loss of provenance), (2) facilitate SSRF-like access to internal resources if the environment permits (e.g., http://localhost, cloud metadata IPs), and (3) download unexpected content types (HTML, large files) under the guise of images. While not a direct exploit by itself, it is a risky tool pattern that can be triggered by attacker-controlled issue content.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  “Download using `curl -sL "url" -o /tmp/image.png` (follow redirects)”
  ```

  **Remediation:** Use a unique, randomized filename per download; validate scheme/host (deny localhost, link-local, private IP ranges); enforce size limits and timeouts; verify content-type/magic bytes before processing; consider an allowlist of trusted attachment hosts (e.g., github.com/user-attachments).

  #### [LOW] Broken references to non-existent scripts/files may cause unsafe improvisation

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** harmful_content
  **Rule ID:** LLM_HARMFUL_CONTENT
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The instructions reference BOTH.py and comments.py, but they are not present. Missing referenced implementation can cause an agent to improvise ad-hoc commands or code to compensate, increasing the risk of unsafe behaviors (e.g., writing new scripts, running unreviewed commands) outside the intended package design.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced Files: “BOTH.py” (not found), “comments.py” (not found)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove stale references or include the referenced files. If the skill expects the agent to create scripts, explicitly constrain what can be generated/executed and require user approval before running any generated code.

  #### [LOW] Capability inflation / ambiguous tool assumptions (requires external tools not declared in manifest)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest omits allowed-tools and compatibility, while the instructions assume broad capabilities (issue tracker access via MCP/API/CLI, authenticated VCS CLI, curl downloads, optional document platform access, transcript tools). This can lead to over-broad activation expectations and increases the chance an agent will attempt risky tool use without explicit scoping or user confirmation.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  “Must be able to fetch the issue details (through MCP, API, or CLI)”
  “Version control CLI (e.g. gh, git): Must be installed and authenticated.”
  “curl: Used to download images.”
  ```

  **Remediation:** Declare allowed-tools explicitly in YAML (e.g., Bash, Read, etc.) and document required integrations. Add a confirmation step before any network/download actions and clarify that authentication tokens/credentials must not be accessed or transmitted.

  **Severity:** CRITICAL
  **Category:** command_injection
  **Rule ID:** LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION

  **Description:** Static analysis indicates multiple Python code blocks in the markdown content use eval/exec. If an agent executes these snippets (or if the skill instructs the agent to run them), any attacker-controlled input flowing into eval/exec can lead to arbitrary code execution, command execution, filesystem access, and potential credential theft. Even without explicit scripts, embedded executable snippets in documentation-style skills are a common execution path in agent workflows.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Pre-scan finding: MDBLOCK_PYTHON_EVAL_EXEC (repeated 5x)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove eval/exec usage.   **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE

  **Description:** The skill references a file (the.py) that is not present. In agent settings, missing referenced artifacts can cause the agent to search elsewhere in the filesystem, fetch substitutes from the network, or accept user-provided replacements—creating opportunities for tool misuse, unintended data access, or execution of untrusted code if the agent attempts to "repair" the missing dependency.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Referenced Files: the.py (not found)
  ```

  **Remediation:** Remove the reference if unnecessary, or include the file in the package. Add explicit instructions to not search outside the skill directory or download replacements from the internet; require user confirmation before using any substitute content.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools/compatibility metadata reduces enforceable safety boundaries

  **Severity:** LOW

  **Description:** The manifest omits allowed-tools and compatibility. While optional, the absence of declared tool constraints makes it harder to enforce least-privilege execution and to detect tool-restriction violations during review, increasing the chance of capability creep in future updates (e.g., adding networked link-auditing behaviors without clear declaration).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  allowed-tools: Not specified; compatibility: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Declare allowed-tools explicitly (e.g., [Read, Grep, Glob] if intended to be non-executing) and document whether network access is expected for link auditing. Keep metadata (including lastUpdated) accurate and validated.

### [OK] goplaces

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 3
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/goplaces

  #### [MEDIUM] File extension does not match actual content type

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** obfuscation
  **Rule ID:** FILE_MAGIC_MISMATCH
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** File 'SKILL.md' extension (.md) suggests one format but Magika detected a different text format: YAML source (yaml). This may indicate content obfuscation or a misnamed file.

  **Remediation:** Rename the file to match its actual content type, or remove it if it appears malicious.

  #### [MEDIUM] Potential sensitive data exposure via environment-variable API key usage and proxyable base URL

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill requires a Google Places API key via the GOOGLE_PLACES_API_KEY environment variable and also supports overriding GOOGLE_PLACES_BASE_URL for testing/proxying. While this is common for CLI tools, it creates a realistic risk that users route requests through an untrusted proxy/base URL, potentially exposing the API key and query contents to third parties. Because the skill is a wrapper around an external CLI that makes network requests, the primary risk is inadvertent credential exposure and data leakage through misconfiguration rather than explicit malicious code in this package.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Config

  - `GOOGLE_PLACES_API_KEY` required.
  - Optional: `GOOGLE_PLACES_BASE_URL` for testing/proxying.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Document safe handling explicitly: warn users not to set GOOGLE_PLACES_BASE_URL to untrusted endpoints; recommend restricting the API key (HTTP referrers/IPs where applicable), using least-privilege keys, and avoiding logging/printing the key. If the skill/package includes any wrapper scripts in the future, ensure they never echo the key and consider validating that the base URL is an expected domain (or require an explicit --allow-custom-base-url acknowledgement).

  **Description:** The manifest omits allowed-tools and compatibility. For a skill that instructs installation and use of a network-calling CLI (Homebrew install; Google Places API queries), the lack of explicit tool constraints and environment/network expectations reduces transparency and can lead to over-broad execution in agent contexts.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  YAML Manifest Details:
  - compatibility: Not specified
  - allowed-tools: Not specified

  Install
  - Homebrew: `brew install steipete/tap/goplaces`
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit manifest metadata: set allowed-tools to the minimum required (likely Bash) and document that the skill performs outbound network requests to Google endpoints (and optionally to a user-specified base URL). ### [OK] tmux-controller

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 3
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/tmux-controller

  #### [MEDIUM] Implicit tool expansion: skill requires Bash/tmux control but manifest omits allowed-tools

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill’s documented usage and included scripts clearly rely on executing shell commands (tmux, grep, sleep, date). However, the YAML manifest does not specify `allowed-tools`. In agent-skill ecosystems, omission can lead to overly broad or ambiguous tool authorization and makes it harder to enforce least-privilege (e.g., restricting to Bash only, or preventing unintended file/network tools). This is not a direct exploit by itself, but it increases the risk of unauthorized tool use in deployments that depend on the manifest for policy enforcement.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Manifest: allowed-tools: Not specified
  Instructions/scripts: extensive `tmux ...` command usage; scripts are Bash and require tmux in PATH.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an explicit `allowed-tools` list in the manifest (at minimum `[Bash]` for these scripts). If the skill is intended to be read-only with respect to the filesystem/network, document that and ensure the agent runtime enforces it.

  #### [LOW] High-impact capability (interactive keystroke injection) without explicit safety/confirmation guidance

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The skill is designed to send keystrokes into existing tmux sessions, which can trigger destructive actions depending on what is running in the pane (e.g., confirming prompts, executing commands in a shell, interacting with privileged sessions). The documentation includes examples that approve prompts (sending 'y' + Enter) and general task dispatch. This is expected functionality, but it is a high-impact capability that can be abused through social engineering or accidental activation if an agent uses it without user confirmation or target validation.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Examples include: `tmux send-keys -t worker-3 'y' Enter` and `tmux send-keys -t worker-4 "Fix the bug in auth.js" Enter`.
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit guardrails in SKILL.md: require confirming the target session/pane, capturing and showing recent output before sending keys, and requiring user confirmation before sending Enter/approval keys. Recommend using fixed, pre-approved targets and avoiding privileged sessions.

  #### [MEDIUM] Potential compute/availability risk via unbounded polling parameters in wait-for-text.sh

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** resource_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE
  **Location:** scripts/wait-for-text.sh

  **Description:** The polling loop in `wait-for-text.sh` runs until timeout, but user-controlled parameters (`--lines`, `--interval`, `--timeout`) can be set to values that cause excessive CPU usage and/or large memory/IO consumption. For example, a very small interval (or 0) combined with a very large `--lines` value can repeatedly capture large scrollback content and grep it, potentially degrading system performance. While `timeout` is bounded by user input, it can still be set very high, effectively creating a long-running busy poll.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  while true; do
    pane_text="$(tmux capture-pane -p -J -t "$target" -S "-${lines}" ... )"
    ...
    sleep "$interval"
  done
  ```

  **Remediation:** Enforce sane bounds: minimum interval (e.g., >=0.2s), maximum lines (e.g., <=5000), and maximum timeout (e.g., <=300s) unless explicitly overridden with a separate "--unsafe" flag. Consider exponential backoff or event-driven alternatives where possible.

  ```
  Description includes multiple trigger categories and broad troubleshooting scope (Tailwind, Shiki, Vite, AI streaming, security).
  ```

  **Remediation:** Narrow the trigger conditions to concrete Streamdown-specific tasks, and add explicit non-goals (e.g., not a general Next.js/Vite troubleshooter). Consider adding a short 'When NOT to use this skill' section.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools metadata (informational)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE

  **Description:** The manifest does not specify allowed-tools. Per the provided framework this is optional, but omitting it reduces clarity about intended tool usage and makes it harder to detect tool-scope violations.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  allowed-tools: Not specified
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add an explicit allowed-tools list consistent with expected operations (likely Read/Write/Grep/Glob and possibly Bash for npm commands, depending on policy).

  #### [MEDIUM] Unreferenced Bash scripts present (potential hidden tool behavior)

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** Static inventory indicates two Bash scripts exist in the package but are not referenced anywhere in SKILL.md instructions. Unreferenced executables are a common place to hide unexpected behaviors (e.g., environment discovery, filesystem traversal, network calls) and can be invoked by an agent even if not documented. Without script contents, risk cannot be fully assessed, but presence alone warrants review and either removal or explicit documentation/justification.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Pre-Scan Context: unreferenced_scripts=['scripts/wait-for-text.sh', 'scripts/find-sessions.sh']
  ```

  **Remediation:** Review both scripts for sensitive operations (filesystem traversal, credential access, network egress, command execution). If benign, reference them explicitly in SKILL.md with clear purpose and safe-usage constraints. If unnecessary, remove them from the skill package. Consider adding an allowlist of intended scripts and CI checks to fail builds when unreferenced executables are present.

  #### [LOW] Skill description exceeds agent skills length limit

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** policy_violation
  **Rule ID:** MANIFEST_DESCRIPTION_TOO_LONG
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** Skill description is 664 characters; Agent skills limit the `description` field to 512 characters.

  **Remediation:** Shorten the description to 512 characters or fewer while keeping it specific

  #### [LOW] Security posture messaging may be misleading if copied without hardening

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** harmful_content
  **Rule ID:** LLM_HARMFUL_CONTENT
  **Location:** references/security.md

  **Description:** SKILL.md claims Streamdown has "built-in ... security" and the security reference states defaults are permissive (all prefixes/protocols/data images allowed) while relying on sanitize/harden/link modal. This can lead users to assume safe-by-default behavior and deploy permissive settings (e.g., allowing data: images, broad URL prefixes) in AI-content contexts. This is primarily a documentation risk rather than an exploit in the skill itself.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  SKILL.md: "built-in streaming support, security"; references/security.md: "permissive by default (all prefixes, protocols, and data images allowed)"
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add a prominent warning in SKILL.md Quick Setup and Security sections: defaults are permissive; for untrusted/AI-generated markdown, provide a strict recommended baseline (protocol allowlist, domain allowlist, disable data: images, consider skipHtml/remove rehype-raw).

  #### [LOW] Numerous referenced files are missing (integrity/maintenance risk)

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** harmful_content
  **Rule ID:** LLM_HARMFUL_CONTENT
  **Location:** references/security.md

  **Description:** Instructions reference many files that are reported as not found (e.g., assets/security.md, multiple templates/_ and references/examples/_). Missing references can cause agents to improvise or fetch external substitutes, increasing the chance of unsafe guidance or indirect prompt injection via external sources (even if not currently instructed).

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```

  ```

  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** SKILL.md suggests configuring an API key in a local JSON config path. This is not inherently insecure, but it can lead to accidental key exposure (e.g., committing config files, permissive file permissions) without guidance on secure storage practices.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  Set OPENAI_API_KEY, or configure it in ~/.the assistant/the assistant.json:

      "openai-whisper-api": {
        apiKey: "OPENAI_KEY_HERE",
      },
    },
  }
  ```

  **Remediation:** Recommend using environment variables or an OS keychain/secret manager; document file permission hardening (e.g., chmod 600) and explicitly warn not to commit config files to version control.

  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE


  #### [MEDIUM] Sensitive audio content is transmitted to a third-party API without explicit user confirmation or redaction guidance

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** data_exfiltration
  **Rule ID:** LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION
  **Location:** scripts/transcribe.sh

  **Description:** The skill uploads the full provided audio file to OpenAI’s /v1/audio/transcriptions endpoint. While this is expected for transcription, the package does not include safeguards or guidance to prevent accidental submission of sensitive recordings (PII, credentials spoken aloud, regulated data). In agent settings, this can become an unintended data exposure path if the agent is asked to transcribe files without the user understanding the external transfer.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  curl -sS https://api.openai.com/v1/audio/transcriptions \
    -H "Authorization: Bearer $OPENAI_API_KEY" \
    -F "file=@${in}" \
    ...
  ```

  **Remediation:** Add explicit warnings in SKILL.md that audio is uploaded to an external service; recommend user confirmation before sending sensitive audio; optionally add a "--confirm" flag or interactive prompt (with a non-interactive override) and/or a policy note to avoid transcribing secrets/regulated data.

### [OK] analytics-tracking

- **Max Severity:** MEDIUM
- **Findings:** 4
- **Directory:** /home/runner/work/awesome-agent-toolbox/awesome-agent-toolbox/catalog/skills/analytics-tracking

  #### [MEDIUM] Path traversal / out-of-package references to tools registry and integrations

  **Severity:** MEDIUM
  **Category:** unauthorized_tool_use
  **Rule ID:** LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The SKILL.md instructions reference multiple files via relative paths that traverse outside the skill package (e.g., ../../tools/REGISTRY.md and ../../tools/integrations/\*.md). In many agent runtimes, allowing a skill to direct the agent to read arbitrary relative paths can enable unauthorized access to host/project files, tool registries, or other sensitive documentation. Even if the current analyzer blocked these reads, the skill design still encourages cross-boundary file access and could be exploitable in less restrictive environments.

  **Code Snippet:**

  ```
  References include: ../../tools/REGISTRY.md, ../../tools/integrations/ga4.md, ../../tools/integrations/mixpanel.md, ../../tools/integrations/amplitude.md, ../../tools/integrations/posthog.md, ../../tools/integrations/segment.md (noted as 'blocked: path traversal attempt' by the scanner).
  ```

  **Remediation:** Avoid using ../ traversal in skill documentation. Vendor/copy required integration guidance into the skill package under a local references/ directory, or require the user to provide explicit file paths. If the platform supports it, enforce a sandbox that restricts file reads to the skill directory and explicitly deny parent-directory traversal.

  #### [LOW] Missing allowed-tools declaration reduces enforceable least-privilege

  **Severity:** LOW
  **Category:** skill_discovery_abuse
  **Rule ID:** LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE
  **Location:** SKILL.md

  **Description:** The manifest does not specify allowed-tools. While optional, omitting it makes it harder to enforce least-privilege expectations for this skill (e.g., whether it should only Read/Grep vs. also Bash/Python). Given the instructions encourage reading local context files, an explicit tool allowlist would improve safety and reduce capability ambiguity.

  **Code Snippet:**

